| DIM-98-003 | DIMENSION_REVIEW |  | H6664G tsedeq and H6663 tsadeq produce four distinct groups: (1) human inner quality [942-001], (2) divine attribute [942-002], (3) pursued inner orientation [942-003], (4) moral basis for governance [942-004]. This fourfold pattern in a single Hebrew root is a major Session B analytical focus. |

**Findings — via anchor verse (64 unique)**

| Finding ID | Type | Matched anchor refs | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| OBS-099-T2-066 | OBSERVATION | Psa 85:10 | Clear pattern established. (a) Strongest semantic co-occurrences: chesed + emet (faithfulness/truth) — persistent OT pairing [XREG: H2617A, OBS-099-089]; chesed + mishpat (justice) — Psa 85:10, Mic 6:8 [OBS-099-038/052]; chrēstotēs + anochē + makrothumia (forbearance + patience) — Rom 2:4 [OBS-099-076]; Gal 5:22 fruit cluster [XREG: G5544, OBS-099-092]. (b) Semantically meaningful verse co-occurrence: Reg 103 (love, 29), Reg 60 (faithfulness, 28), Reg 98 (justice, 27) [OBS-099-037]. (Note: high counts Reg 197/187/61 are am-variant group driven, not semantic kinship.) (c) Cluster: kindness clusters tightly with faithfulness, justice, humility, patience, compassion — forming C17 relational-grace cluster's core vocabulary. |
| OBS-117-T2-004 | OBSERVATION | Psa 85:10 | The evidence shows both. (1) Peace as part of original design: Psa 85:10 ("righteousness and peace kiss each other") presents the eschatological restoration as a reunion of characteristics that belong together — implying they were originally united. The messianic reign of peace (Isa 9:6-7, Mic 5:5, OBS-117-147) is framed as the restoration of what was intended, not the creation of something novel. (2) Peace as response to the fallen condition: the "covenant of peace" structure (Num 25:12, Isa 54:10, Eze 34:25/37:26, OBS-117-145) presupposes a broken relationship being restored — covenant peace is given because natural peace has been ruptured. Isa 53:5 ("the chastisement that brought us peace, and with his wounds we are healed") explicitly frames peace as purchased healing of a rupture. Gap: the evidence does not directly address the pre-fall condition — Scripture's earliest peace statements (Gen 28:21, Gen 37:4) already presuppose the fallen world. The precise relationship between created peace and covenant-restored peace requires Session D synthesis with other registries. |
| OBS-117-T2-023 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Psa 35:27 | The immediate responses to shalom's presence documented in the data: (1) Rest and sleep — Psa 4:8 ("in peace I will both lie down and sleep"); shalom immediately enables the cessation of vigilance; (2) Absence of fear — Judg 6:23 ("Peace be to you. Do not fear") — OBS-117-144; the divine peace immediately displaces terror; (3) Gladness and praise — Psa 35:27 ("shout for joy and be glad... Great is the Lord, who delights in the welfare of his servant") — OBS-117-149; shalom's presence triggers praise; (4) Heart-joining — 1Ch 12:17 ("my heart will be joined to you") — OBS-117-151; shalom's presence in friendship produces heart-union; (5) Daniel's strengthening — Dan 10:19 ("peace be with you; be strong and of good courage") — the angelic peace immediately produces inner strength. The pattern: shalom's immediate effect is the displacement of its contraries (fear, vigilance, inner fragmentation) and the enabling of their alternatives (rest, praise, courage, heart-union). |
| OBS-117-T2-066 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Psa 35:27 | Yes — extensively. Peace is itself primarily an experiential/affective condition in many of its manifestations. The Emotion-Positive dimension assignments (groups 2508-001, 2509-001, 419-002, 418-004, 2585-001, 2585-002, and others) directly name the affective dimension. Key affective evidence: Psa 4:8 — lying down and sleeping in peace (the affect of deep security); Psa 35:27 — shout for joy and be glad, because of the Lord's delight in welfare (joy triggered by shalom); Jer 30:10 — "Jacob shall return and have quiet and ease, and none shall make him afraid" — ease as the affective state of the peacefully restored. The affect-peace connection is foundational: peace is, at the affective level, the felt condition of security, rest, welfare, and the absence of fear. |
| OBS-117-T2-090 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Psa 35:27 | God's extension of peace reveals his constitutive orientation of goodwill and wholeness toward the human person. Three dimensions: (1) God delights in the welfare of his servant (Psa 35:27 — OBS-117-149) — peace-giving is the expression of divine delight, not merely duty; (2) God plans for welfare and not for evil (Jer 29:11) — the divine intention toward the human person is purposively peaceful; (3) God identified as peace (YHWH-Shalom, 406-001) — God's own identity includes peace as a constitutive attribute, meaning his extension of peace is not situational but essential to who he is. The convergence of these three dimensions reveals that God's peace-giving is not a response to human deserving but the overflow of his own identity — the God who IS peace gives peace because that is what he is. |
| OBS-060-T2-001 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | The verse evidence reveals that giving (na.tan) is constitutive of God's character in multiple modes: God gives from covenantal faithfulness (OBS-060-018, OBS-060-035, OBS-060-036), from compassion responding to suffering (OBS-060-037, OBS-060-038), from anger in moral judgement (OBS-060-043), and from eschatological generosity (OBS-060-048, OBS-060-049). The anchor verses disclose that divine giving is never transactional — it is always the expression of an inner-being orientation. God gives land as the enactment of his covenantal word (Gen 12:7, Gen 15:18); God gives Spirit to instruct as the expression of his formative care (Neh 9:20); God gives eschatological transformation from depletion to praise (Isa 61:3). The character of God reflected in this registry is: a God whose inner commitments, compassions, and purposes necessarily express themselves as giving. Giving is the outward form of God's inner faithfulness. |
| OBS-060-T2-002 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | Yes, and extensively so. Six of seven groups have GOD as dominant subject — God is the giver in the overwhelming majority of classified verses (OBS-060-017). More specifically, anchor verses attribute inner-being vocabulary to God in connection with giving: leb (heart) recoils in compassion before giving-over is withheld (Hos 11:8 — OBS-060-038); nephesh (soul) is invoked — "beloved of my soul" — preceding the giving-over in judgement (Jer 12:7 — OBS-060-042). This attribution reveals that giving in the inner-being register is grounded first in God — it is something God does from within before it is something the human does. For the human person, the significance is: inner-being giving (self-giving, sacrificial giving, votive giving) is a creaturely participation in a divine mode of action. Human giving that reflects God's character is not merely morally good — it is image-bearing. |
| OBS-060-T2-004 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | Yes — group 872-007 (Transformation, OBS-060-024) and its anchors demonstrate this directly. Isa 61:3 shows giving as eschatological reversal: the faint spirit receives a garment of praise; mourning becomes gladness; ashes become beauty (OBS-060-048, OBS-060-049). The giving exceeds restoration — it creates a new inner-being state not previously possessed. Hos 2:15 (OBS-060-050) shows the shame-site becoming a door of hope; the silenced inner responsiveness restored to singing. These are not descriptions of the human as currently constituted but of where divine giving is taking the inner person. The orientation toward fullness is explicit: "oaks of righteousness, the planting of the Lord, that he may be glorified" (Isa 61:3) — the endpoint is an identity and orientation that does not yet fully exist in the present condition. |
| OBS-060-T2-008 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | Yes, at two levels. Covenantal-eschatological: group 872-007 and anchor Isa 61:3 (OBS-060-048, OBS-060-049) deploy giving typologically — the eschatological giving exceeds what any immediate provision can achieve; it points toward a new-creation reality where the faint spirit becomes a praising one. Hos 2:15 deploys the Valley of Achor (a site of past shame) as a typological reversal: the history of failure becomes the entrance to hope, re-enacting the Exodus pattern (OBS-060-050). Christological: Isa 50:6 (OBS-060-045) is the Servant's bodily self-giving — widely read in the canonical context as pointing toward the Servant's death as vicarious self-offering. The verse's inner-being structure (giving the body from an inner disposition of trust) carries typological weight regardless of the interpretive decision about the referent. |
| OBS-060-T2-009 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | The direction is primarily divine-to-human: the divine instance establishes the pattern that the human instance echoes and participates in. God's covenantal giving (872-001) is the pattern; human giving from inner orientation (872-005) is the echo (OBS-060-008, Q&A-008). God's eschatological giving (872-007) establishes the pattern of what restoration looks like; the human moves toward it as recipient and, ultimately, as participant in the praise-giving that results ("garment of praise" — the human becomes a giver of praise in response). The one exception is potentially Isa 50:6 — the Servant's bodily self-giving — where the human/Servant instance may carry typological weight pointing toward a divine act. In that case the direction would be human → divine, but this remains a contested interpretive determination. The dominant structural direction in the data is: divine giving pattern → human giving response. |
| OBS-060-T2-014 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The characteristic is structurally complex — it has at minimum three constituent elements visible in the data. (1) An inner-being source: the orientation, commitment, or emotional state from which the giving proceeds (covenant faithfulness, compassion, anger, trust, obedience). (2) The act of giving itself: na.tan in its specific contextual configuration (bestowing, endowing, giving-over, self-offering, glory-ascribing). (3) The object given: what is transferred determines which dimension of faithfulness is expressed (land/promise → covenantal faithfulness; spirit/wisdom → formative endowment; glory → correspondence; praise/gladness → eschatological transformation). These three elements are always present in the classified verses and always in relationship — the source determines the act determines the object. This triadic structure (source → act → object) is the constituent architecture of faithfulness-as-giving in the data. |
| OBS-060-T2-015 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Working description: Faithfulness in this registry is the inner-being quality by which a person (primarily God, secondarily the human image-bearer) enacts their inner commitments, orientations, and compassions through giving — giving that is always relational, directed, and sourced in an inner-being state rather than in external obligation. It operates at the intersection of moral character (covenant keeping), relational disposition (compassion enacted), cognition (endowing inner capacities), transformation (eschatological reversal of inner states), and divine-human correspondence (glory-ascription in its proper direction). The primary faculty engagement is the whole person acting from within: the heart (leb) as source (Hos 11:8), the soul (nephesh) as the seat of attachment that motivates giving (Jer 12:7), the spirit (ruach) as both the object of endowing-giving (Neh 9:20) and the faculty transformed by eschatological-giving (Isa 61:3). Impact on the person: receiving divine inner-being giving produces trust, praise, transformed inner states, and restored responsiveness; giving from inner orientation produces alignment with God and participation in the divine pattern of faithful action. |
| OBS-060-T2-019 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The data reveals five distinct operational modes (OBS-060-025, OBS-060-051). (1) Performative giving: the act of giving constitutes a covenantal reality — "I will give this land" enacts the covenant (OBS-060-016, OBS-060-036). (2) Responsive giving: giving triggered by the recipient's inner condition — God gives in direct answer to suffering/need (OBS-060-037). (3) Endowing giving: giving that deposits inner-being capacity — Spirit, wisdom, understanding given directly to the inner life (OBS-060-040, OBS-060-041). (4) Judicial giving: giving as moral consequence — giving-over as the expression of inner moral response to faithlessness (OBS-060-042, OBS-060-043). (5) Transformative giving: giving that reverses inner-being states — replacing depletion with fullness, mourning with gladness (OBS-060-048, OBS-060-049, OBS-060-050). In the human direction, a sixth mode is also present: (6) Relinquishing giving: releasing what was received, recognising divine sovereignty over all given goods (OBS-060-044). |
| OBS-060-T2-020 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Yes — significantly so. By direction: divine giving (6 of 7 groups) operates from sovereign initiative and covenantal obligation; human giving (1 group, 872-005) operates from responsive orientation and trust. The operational logic differs: God gives as the originating party; the human gives as the responsive, image-bearing party. By context: performative giving (covenant ratification, Gen 15:18) operates as a speech-act constituting reality; responsive giving (Gen 29:33) operates as compassionate provision; judicial giving (Hos 13:11) operates as moral consequence; transformative giving (Isa 61:3) operates as eschatological reversal. By constitutional level: endowing giving (Neh 9:20, Job 38:36) operates at the spirit/cognitive level, depositing inner-being capacity; self-giving (Isa 50:6) operates through the body as the expression of inner disposition; glory-giving (Isa 42:8, Jer 13:16) operates at the relational-correspondence level between inner-being orientation and its proper object. |
| OBS-060-T2-025 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | The sustained inner-being effects of divine giving in this registry cluster around three outcomes. (1) Identity formation: God's covenantal giving over generations produces a people who know themselves as recipients of promise — the land-giving texts (Gen 12:7 through Jer 17:4 in group 872-001) address communities who inherit a narrative of divine faithfulness accumulated across generations. (2) Inner-being capacity: the endowing giving (872-003) deposits Spirit, wisdom, and understanding that shape the person's cognitive and formative capacities over time (Neh 9:20, OBS-060-040). (3) Transformed inner states: the eschatological giving (872-007) produces lasting new inner-being orientations — not momentary experience but stable conditions: "oaks of righteousness" (Isa 61:3, OBS-060-049) names a durable identity, not a passing moment. |
| OBS-060-T2-026 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Sustained divine giving establishes: (a) Trust — the pattern of divine covenantal giving over generations grounds the expectation that God will continue to give as promised (group 872-001 corpus). (b) Praise orientation — Isa 61:3 names the endpoint as "garment of praise" (OBS-060-048) — sustained eschatological giving produces a stable praise-orientation as the characteristic inner-being state. (c) Responsiveness — Hos 2:15 ("she shall answer") names restored inner responsiveness as the sustained outcome (OBS-060-050). (d) Cognitive formation — sustained Spirit-giving and wisdom-giving produce shaped understanding and discernment over time (Neh 9:20, OBS-060-040). For the human giving outward: sustained practice of inner-being giving (self-giving, votive giving, glory-giving) produces the stable disposition that the group descriptions name — an inner orientation toward God and community that becomes characteristic of the person. |
| OBS-060-T2-027 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Yes — the sustained effect differs from the immediate response in the data. The immediate response is directional and affective: worship, blessing, naming (Gen 12:7, Gen 29:33, Job 1:21). The sustained effect is constitutional: identity as recipients of covenant promise, formed cognitive capacity, stable praise-orientation, restored responsiveness. The immediate response is an inner-being movement (toward God, in acknowledgement); the sustained effect is an inner-being state (who the person has become through accumulated receiving). The distinction is between the first response to receiving (immediate) and the person shaped by a pattern of receiving over time (sustained). This is consistent with the distinction between T1.5 and T1.6 being deliberately kept separate in the catalogue design — the two are structurally different and the data confirms they should not be conflated. |
| OBS-060-T2-028 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | The data provides partial evidence. For covenantal giving (872-001): the condition evidenced is being in a covenant relationship with God — the giving of land is addressed to Abraham and his offspring, not to all humanity indiscriminately. The covenant relationship is the precondition of reception. For compassionate giving (872-002): the condition evidenced is the presenting of genuine inner need — Leah is hated and suffering; God hears and gives (OBS-060-037). Expressed need or suffering appears to enable responsive giving. For eschatological giving (872-007): no precondition is named in the anchor verses — those who mourn receive beauty (Isa 61:3), suggesting mourning itself is the enabling condition. For human self-giving (Job 1:21, Isa 50:6): the enabling inner condition is trust/covenantal identity — Job's worship proceeds from recognising divine sovereignty; the Servant gives himself because he trusts divine vindication. Gap: the data does not address whether ongoing faithfulness or human moral condition affects the receptivity to divine covenantal giving — the texts name the giving as God's initiative, not as contingent on human merit. |
| OBS-060-T2-033 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Yes — four secondary dimensions are carried, each with substantial group-level evidence: (1) Relational Disposition (groups 872-002 and 872-005) — compassionate responsive giving (GOD) and self-giving as inner orientation (HUMAN). This dimension is the most quantitatively significant after Moral Character (30 + 19 = 49 relevant verses combined). (2) Cognition (group 872-003) — divine giving of inner-being capacities: Spirit, wisdom, understanding. (3) Divine-Human Correspondence (group 872-006) — glory-ascription in its proper directional structure. (4) Transformation (group 872-007) — eschatological reversal of inner states. These secondary dimensions do not compete with Moral Character as primary — they are distinct operational modes of the same characteristic, each revealing a different dimension of how inner-being giving functions in the biblical corpus. The DIM-60-SD001 pointer (OBS-060-033) identifies this dimensional breadth as a programme-level finding: na.tan illustrates that a single action-verb can carry the full breadth of inner-being dimensional engagement depending on context. |
| OBS-060-T2-034 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | Isa 61:3 | Yes — in two distinct modes. (1) As the object of divine giving: Neh 9:20 (OBS-060-040) — "You gave your good Spirit to instruct them" — the ruach is given to the human inner life to form it cognitively. The Spirit is the content of God's endowing gift. (2) As the faculty transformed by eschatological giving: Isa 61:3 (OBS-060-048) — "garment of praise instead of a faint spirit (ruach kehah)" — the human spirit in its depleted state is the target of eschatological giving, resulting in transformation to a praise-orientation. In both cases ruach appears in explicit connection with na.tan: in Neh 9:20 as the object given; in Isa 61:3 as the faculty undergoing transformation through giving. |
| OBS-060-T2-035 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | Isa 61:3 | The evidence is mixed. The endowing mode (872-003) is clearly spirit-level in its object: God gives the Spirit to the human inner life, and this giving deposits capacity at the spirit level (Neh 9:20, OBS-060-040). The eschatological mode (872-007) transforms the spirit directly (Isa 61:3). However, the other five groups do not show explicit spirit-level origination: the covenantal giving (872-001) and compassionate giving (872-002) operate from the divine heart and compassion (leb, rahamim — Hos 11:8, OBS-060-038), which are not identified as spirit-level in this data. The judicial giving (872-004) proceeds from divine anger/wrath — emotion-level, not spirit-level. Human self-giving (872-005) is not explicitly located at the spirit level in the anchor verses. The glory-giving (872-006) operates at the relational-correspondence level without explicit spirit-level identification. Assessment: spirit-level location is evidenced in two of seven groups; it is not the primary constitutional home of the characteristic across the full corpus. |
| OBS-060-T2-036 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | Isa 61:3 | Where spirit-level location is evidenced, it reveals two things about the depth of this characteristic. (1) Giving at the spirit level is the deepest form of endowment: when God gives Spirit to instruct (Neh 9:20), the giving reaches into the foundational constitutional layer of the human person — forming the inner life from the inside out. This is not provision of external goods but constitution of inner capacity. (2) The faint spirit (ruach kehah, Isa 61:3) as the target of eschatological transformation reveals that the human spirit can be depleted — its vitality reduced — and that this depletion is the condition the transformative giving addresses. The eschatological gift therefore operates at the deepest level: renewing what is most fundamental to the person's inner-being vitality. Both instances confirm that spirit-level giving is the most constitutionally significant mode in the registry. |
| OBS-060-T2-050 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | Isa 61:3 | Supporting generational transmission (not body-deposit per se): the covenant-giving texts address Abraham's offspring across multiple generations — Gen 12:7, Gen 17:8, Gen 26:3, Neh 9:35-36. The land-giving is inherited identity, not re-given to each individual. Contradicting constitutional body-deposit: the Spirit-giving in Neh 9:20 is presented as a specific historical act to the wilderness generation, not as a permanent somatic inheritance. The eschatological giving in Isa 61:3 and Hos 2:15 is presented as new initiative, not as retrieval of an inherited deposit. The evidence does not support a constitutional body deposit in the somatic sense; what transmits generationally is covenantal identity and promise, which is relational-narratival rather than biological. |
| OBS-060-T2-055 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | Isa 61:3 | Yes — constitutional movement is evidenced in multiple directions across the groups. (1) Spirit → expression: Neh 9:20 — God gives Spirit to instruct; the Spirit-giving operates at the spirit level and produces cognitive-formational capacity at the soul/mind level. Spirit → mind movement. (2) Inner → body: Isa 50:6 — inner disposition (trust, covenantal obedience) expressed through bodily surfaces (back, cheeks, face). Inner → body movement. (3) Body → inner (divine constitution): Isa 42:5 — God gives breath (neshamah) to constitute bodily life; the bodily breath-gift underlies the capacity for inner-being existence. (4) Eschatological: Isa 61:3 — the faint spirit receives a garment of praise; the spirit-level depletion is transformed into an embodied-inner orientation (garment = outer expression of inner state). Spirit ↔ body movement in the eschatological register. |
| OBS-060-T2-056 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | Isa 61:3 | Two distinct movement patterns are evidenced. (1) The endowing pattern: divine giving moves from spirit level downward and outward — Spirit given → understanding formed in the hidden inner parts → person equipped to instruct/act. The sequence is constitutive: each level is enabled by the gift at the deeper level. (2) The expressive pattern: inner orientation (trust, covenantal commitment) moves from inner to outer — inner disposition → bodily act (Isa 50:6) or visible response (altar-building, Gen 12:7) or praise (Job 1:21). The sequence is expressive: the inner is prior, the outer is derivative. (3) The eschatological pattern: external divine act transforms internal state — God gives eschatologically → faint spirit becomes praising spirit → outer garment of praise expresses transformed inner state. The sequence is transformative: outside-in → inside renewed → inside-out again. These three patterns (constitutive, expressive, transformative) represent the full movement architecture of the characteristic. |
| OBS-060-T2-066 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Yes — prominently. The affective faculty is engaged in multiple groups. (1) Divine affect as source of giving: Hos 11:8 (OBS-060-038) — compassion (rahamim) grows warm and tender, arresting the giving-over. The divine affective movement is the determinant of giving. Hos 13:11 (OBS-060-043) — anger and wrath as the inner-being states from which judicial giving proceeds. (2) Affect as the object of eschatological giving: Isa 61:3 (OBS-060-048) — mourning replaced by gladness (oil of gladness); sorrow replaced by praise. Affect (mourning, gladness) is both the presenting inner state and the transformed inner state — the eschatological giving directly addresses and transforms emotional experience. (3) Human affect in self-giving: Job 1:21 — blessing under devastating loss is an affective-volitional act. |
| OBS-060-T2-067 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The characteristic both enables and transforms affect. In its eschatological mode (872-007): the transformative giving directly replaces negative affect (mourning, faint spirit) with positive affect (gladness, praise) — Isa 61:3 (OBS-060-048). This is direct affective transformation: the giving enables new affective states that were previously absent or depleted. In its responsive mode (872-002): the divine affective state (rahamim, compassion — Hos 11:8, OBS-060-038) is itself what the giving expresses — the characteristic does not bypass affect but is grounded in it. The human relinquishing mode (Job 1:21, OBS-060-044) shows affect operating under the characteristic: blessing and worship under devastating loss is an affective act, not a stoic suppression of affect. The characteristic does not impair affect in any evidenced instance; where it fails to operate (glory withheld from God), affect is disordered (Jer 13:16 — darkness, stumbling). Affect is constitutively engaged throughout. |
| OBS-060-T2-068 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The pattern reveals that this characteristic is affectively saturated at both ends — in its source (divine compassion, anger) and in its target (mourning transformed to gladness). Giving in the inner-being register is never affectively neutral: it proceeds from inner-being emotional states and it produces or transforms inner-being emotional states. This distinguishes it from transactional giving, which may be affectively indifferent. The characteristic reveals that genuine giving — giving as the expression of inner-being commitment — is constitutively affective: it cannot be separated from love, compassion, anger (in the judicial mode), or grief. The person who gives rightly in this registry is always giving from somewhere deep. The eschatological-transformative mode further reveals that one purpose of divine giving is to heal affective disorder — to restore the inner-being emotional life to its proper orientation. |
| OBS-060-T2-072 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Yes — agency is the primary external form of this characteristic. Every instance of na.tan in the inner-being register is an act — something is given, transferred, offered. Agency is intrinsic: the giving requires an agent who initiates and performs the transfer. In the divine direction: God acts to give land (Gen 12:7), Spirit (Neh 9:20), transformative gifts (Isa 61:3) — all are divine initiatives, sovereign acts of divine agency. In the human direction: Job acts (blesses — Job 1:21), the Servant acts (gives his back — Isa 50:6), humans are commanded to act (give glory — Jer 13:16). The characteristic is not merely a state or disposition — it is always an acted-out disposition. Agency is the faculty through which the inner-being orientation becomes external and real. |
| OBS-060-T2-073 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The endowing giving (872-003) enables agency: Spirit-giving enables the capacity to instruct (Neh 9:20 — sakal). The giving of the tongue of the taught (Isa 50:4) enables the agentive act of sustaining the weary. The cognitive gifts (wisdom, understanding — Job 38:36) enable discerning action. In the eschatological mode: the transformation from faint spirit to garment of praise (Isa 61:3) restores the inner-being vitality that makes agentive participation possible — the depressed, depleted inner life cannot give glory; the restored spirit can and does. The characteristic enables and restores agency rather than bypassing it. The judicial giving-over (872-004) might be read as impairing the recipient's agency — being delivered into enemies' hands removes freedom of action — but this is consequence, not direct engagement of the agency faculty. |
| OBS-060-T2-074 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Agency is not incidental to the characteristic — it is constitutive. The disposition-in-action structure (Q&A-016) requires agency: the inner-being orientation only becomes faithfulness when it produces action. The pattern reveals that this characteristic exists at the intersection of inner orientation and outward act — agency is the bridge. The endowing giving further reveals that where agency is depleted or absent (faint spirit, darkened perception), divine giving restores it. This implies that the characteristic has a self-sustaining property in the divine direction: God's giving enables the human capacity to respond and give in turn, which sustains the relational dynamic. |
| OBS-060-T2-076 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The endowing giving (872-003) enables moral evaluation: wisdom and understanding given to the inward parts (Job 38:36) constitute the cognitive capacity for right moral discernment. Spirit given to instruct (Neh 9:20) forms the moral-cognitive faculty. The eschatological giving restores the inner-being orientation from which moral evaluation flows — "oaks of righteousness" (Isa 61:3) names a stable moral identity restored by the giving. Covenantal giving (872-001) deepens moral evaluation by grounding the person's identity in the narrative of divine faithfulness — the memory of God's reliable giving provides the moral framework against which actions are assessed. In the failure direction: the judicial giving-over (872-004) may represent a consequence of failed moral evaluation (Israel's failure to assess rightly what faithfulness required), though the data is more explicit about the consequence than the prior failure. |
| OBS-060-T2-080 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | The pattern reveals that this characteristic engages conscience indirectly rather than directly: it is the framework (Spirit-giving, prophetic warning-giving, covenantal narrative) within which conscience is formed and addressed, rather than conscience itself. The judicial giving-over is the consequence of rejected conscience-engagement — when divine warning is not received, the trajectory moves toward giving-over. This reveals that inner-being giving is conscience-forming in its endowing and covenantal modes: it builds the inner moral environment within which conscience can function rightly. The characteristic is therefore not primarily a conscience-engaging act but a conscience-constituting one — the giving that forms the moral inner life from which conscience operates. |
| OBS-060-T2-087 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | Yes — extensively and across five of seven groups. God gives covenantally toward Israel (872-001: land, promise, blessing — Gen 12:7, Gen 15:18); compassionately toward those in need (872-002: responsive giving to suffering — Gen 29:33, Hos 11:8 withheld giving-over); formatively toward the inner life (872-003: Spirit, wisdom, understanding — Neh 9:20, Job 38:36); judicially toward the faithless (872-004: giving-over as moral consequence — Hos 13:11, Jer 12:7); and transformatively toward the mourning (872-007: ashes → beauty, faint spirit → praise — Isa 61:3, Hos 2:15). Every mode of God-to-human giving is evidenced in the anchor verses. The God-to-human direction is the dominant directional structure of the registry (6 of 7 groups, OBS-060-017). |
| OBS-060-T2-088 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | The data reveals four distinct bases operating across the groups: (1) Covenantal (872-001): God gives because the covenant is in force — the giving enacts prior covenantal commitment, regardless of immediate merit. Gen 15:18 — the covenant ratification is the basis; the giving follows from it. Mal 2:5 — "my covenant with him was one of life and peace, and I gave them to him" — the giving is covenantal through and through. (2) Responsive (872-002): God gives in response to presented inner need — Leah's condition triggers divine hearing and giving (Gen 29:33). The basis is the creature's condition, perceived by divine attentiveness. (3) Formative (872-003): God gives Spirit/wisdom as constitutive endowment — the basis appears to be divine formative purpose for the person, not contingent on their current state. (4) Moral-consequential (872-004): God gives-over as moral response to faithlessness — the basis is moral consistency: the covenant is executed in both directions. (5) Eschatological-grace (872-007): God gives to the mourning without stated precondition — grace and eschatological initiative are the basis. No single basis covers all modes; the characteristic operates across a spectrum from pure grace to moral consequence. |
| OBS-060-T2-089 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | God's giving reveals a complex of inner-being dispositions toward the human person. (1) Covenantal commitment: God's giving of land and promise over generations reveals that his orientation toward Abraham and his offspring is one of binding, persistent faithfulness — a commitment that does not lapse with time or circumstance. (2) Attentive compassion: God hears Leah's inner condition and gives in response — his orientation is one of attentiveness to the inner suffering of the creature, not detachment. (3) Formative care: God gives Spirit and wisdom to equip the person for formation and service — his orientation is developmental, not merely transactional. (4) Moral seriousness: God gives-over in response to faithlessness — his orientation includes moral consistency and covenantal accountability. He is not sentimentally lenient. (5) Hos 11:8 holds all of these in tension: the heart that recoils in compassion is also the heart that had determined judgement — God's orientation is a complex of justice and compassion held together by sovereign grace. |
| OBS-060-T2-096 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Yes — every group with God as subject shows the human receiving divine inner-being giving. Reception operates differently by mode: (1) Covenantal reception (872-001): the human receives the promise/land as participant in an inherited covenant — reception is identity-constituting. Abram receives the promise and immediately responds with worship (Gen 12:7, OBS-060-035). (2) Compassionate reception (872-002): Leah receives a child in direct response to her inner suffering — reception is responsive and experiential (Gen 29:33, OBS-060-037). (3) Formative reception (872-003): the human receives Spirit, wisdom, and understanding — reception is constitutive, forming inner capacity. (4) Eschatological reception (872-007): those who mourn receive beauty/gladness/praise — reception is transformative, replacing depleted inner states with new ones. The mode of reception corresponds directly to the mode of giving: the characteristic is received as it is given. |
| OBS-060-T2-097 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Enabling conditions for reception (drawn from Q&A-031 and the anchor verse pattern): (1) Covenant participation: being within the relational structure that God's giving constitutes. The land-giving texts presuppose covenant membership as the reception condition. (2) Presented inner need: compassionate giving is triggered by visible inner suffering (Leah's condition, Gen 29:33) — the authentic expression of need enables responsive reception. (3) Posture of mourning/depletion: the eschatological giving is addressed to "those who mourn in Zion" (Isa 61:3) — the state of acknowledged need and depleted inner life. Blocking conditions: (1) Idolatrous misattribution — receiving the gift but attributing it to Baal (Hos 2:8, Q&A-032) blocks genuine reception even when the material gift arrives. (2) Covenant violation — faithlessness inverts giving-to into giving-over (Q&A-032), removing the receptive condition. (3) Refusal to give ear — Neh 9:30 — the rejection of prophetic warning closes the channel of Spirit-mediated reception. |
| OBS-060-T2-099 | OBSERVATION | Isa 42:6 | The evidence shows the characteristic operating primarily within existing relational bonds — the covenantal relationship is the primary relational context of almost all classified giving. The land-giving texts (872-001) presuppose the Abrahamic covenant as the established relational bond. The compassionate giving (872-002) operates within the relationship between God and his people or God and individual sufferers who call upon him. The judicial giving-over (872-004) — Jer 12:7, Hos 13:11 — operates within the covenant relationship precisely because the violation is a covenant violation. However: Isa 42:6 (group 872-006 corpus) — "I will give you as a covenant for the people, a light for the nations" — shows the giving crossing relational boundaries (to the nations), mediated through the covenant servant. The characteristic can operate across relational distance through covenantal mediation. |
| OBS-060-T2-100 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3, Isa 42:6 | Primarily within covenantal contexts but with boundary-crossing capacity through mediation. The dominant mode (872-001) is explicitly covenantal. However, the compassionate giving (872-002) includes instances where the relational basis is need rather than formal covenant: Leah's condition (Gen 29:33) is within the covenant community but the giving is responsive to her individual inner suffering, not to her covenant membership per se. Isa 61:3 addresses "those who mourn in Zion" — the covenantal community, but the giving is to the condition of mourning, not to a specific covenant transaction. Isa 42:6 — "a covenant for the people, a light for the nations" — the servant mediates the giving beyond the covenant boundary. The characteristic operates primarily within covenant but is not constitutionally limited to it. |
| OBS-060-T2-101 | OBSERVATION | Isa 42:6 | The primary scope is the covenantal community — Abraham and his offspring (872-001 corpus), Israel and her sub-communities (Nehemiah, Hosea, Isaiah texts). Within this scope: individuals in need (Leah), the wilderness generation, the mourning in Zion, the depressed Servant. The judicial giving-over (872-004) includes Ephraim/Israel and the nations used as instruments of judgement, expanding the scope negatively. Excluded from covenantal giving: those outside the covenant relationship who have not received its basis (the nations receive the characteristic only through mediation — Isa 42:6). God's non-giving of glory to carved idols (Isa 42:8) establishes the limit at the divine level: idols are explicitly excluded from receiving divine glory-giving. The scope is defined by the relational structure: those in covenant receive covenantal giving; those outside receive at most mediated giving. |
| OBS-060-T2-104 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Yes — the characteristic produces transformation in both modes across different groups. (1) Condition change: eschatological giving (872-007) changes inner-being conditions directly — ashes replaced by beauty, mourning replaced by gladness, faint spirit replaced by garment of praise (Isa 61:3, OBS-060-048). This is condition change: the person's inner state is materially different after receiving the giving. Hos 2:15 — the Valley of Achor transformed into a door of hope — changes both the external condition (the place) and the inner-being condition (shame → hope). (2) Orientation change: Job 1:21 — Job's condition (devastation) does not change through the giving, but his orientation to it changes — blessing under loss is an orientation-to-condition change. The covenantal giving (872-001) changes the condition of landlessness to land-possession while also forming the orientation of covenant identity. The evidence shows both modes: condition change (primary in 872-007) and orientation change (primary in 872-005, also present in 872-001). |
| OBS-060-T2-105 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | The eschatological transformations (Isa 61:3, Hos 2:15) are presented as permanent and final — "oaks of righteousness," "everlasting name" (Isa 56:5), "everlasting covenant" (Isa 61:8). The language of eschatological giving uses permanent-state vocabulary, suggesting irreversibility. The covenantal giving of land is presented as permanently intended ("everlasting possession," Gen 17:8) but historically the people lose the land (Jer 17:4 — "you shall loosen your hand from your heritage that I gave you") — suggesting that even given-land can be forfeited. The gap: the data does not clearly distinguish whether the reversibility of loss (land forfeited) represents a reversal of the transformation or a suspension of one mode of giving while another (judicial giving-over) replaces it. Partial answer: eschatological giving appears irreversible; covenantal giving of material goods appears conditionally reversible. |
| OBS-060-T2-106 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Yes — Isa 61:3 provides the clearest before-during-after sequence in the corpus. Before: ashes, mourning, faint spirit — states of inner-being depletion and grief. During: the act of divine giving ("to give them..."). After: beautiful headdress, oil of gladness, garment of praise — states of inner-being renewal and orientation. Hos 11:8 provides a divine inner-being sequence: before — the judicial logic of giving-over (deserved consequence); during — the heart recoiling, compassion warming; after — the withholding of giving-over, the relational faithfulness expressed. Hos 2:15 provides: before — Valley of Achor (shame, failure); during — divine giving of vineyards; after — door of hope, restored responsiveness ("she shall answer"). The sequences are distinct by mode: eschatological sequences are internal to the human; the compassionate-withholding sequence is internal to God. |
| OBS-060-T2-107 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | The sequences reveal a consistent pattern: the characteristic enters at the point of depletion or need, provides something that the depleted state cannot generate, and produces a new inner-being orientation. The before-state is always one of lack or crisis: grief, faint spirit, shame, threat of giving-over. The after-state is always qualitatively different from a simple restoration — oaks of righteousness is not the pre-grief state recovered but a new identity; the door of hope is not the pre-Achor state but a transformed site; the garment of praise is not the restoration of ordinary vitality but an eschatologically new orientation. This reveals how the characteristic works: it does not reverse the before-state by undoing it — it transforms through it, producing an after-state that incorporates the before-state into something new. The depth of the giving matches the depth of the need. |
| OBS-060-T2-108 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | The evidence shows three distinct mechanisms operating across the groups. (1) Encounter/event: the covenantal giving (Gen 12:7, Gen 15:18) is event-based — a specific divine appearance and speech-act constitutes the giving and its transformative effect. The encounter is sudden and definitional. (2) Gradual formation: the endowing mode (Neh 9:20 — Spirit given to instruct) implies sustained formative engagement — Spirit-guided instruction shapes the inner-cognitive life over time. This is gradual formation rather than sudden transformation. (3) Eschatological reversal: Isa 61:3 and Hos 2:15 operate through direct divine act — the giving replaces the prior state without a formative process. The mechanism is sovereign initiative producing immediate inner-being change. Hosea 11:8 (the heart recoiling) shows a divine inner-being mechanism of self-determined restraint — not discipline or encounter but the assertion of compassion over logic. |
| OBS-060-T2-109 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Yes — the mechanisms are context-specific and correspond directly to the group structure. Covenantal contexts (872-001): encounter/event mechanism — the divine appearance and speech-act is the mechanism. Endowing contexts (872-003): gradual formation — Spirit and wisdom work over time through instruction and application. Eschatological contexts (872-007): sovereign reversal — no process, direct divine initiative replacing one state with another. Judicial contexts (872-004): moral consequence — the mechanism is the enacted consequence of prior moral trajectory, not a transformative encounter. The data confirms that the mechanism varies by mode and cannot be reduced to a single transformative pathway. The characteristic's dimensional breadth (T1.8.3, Q&A-036) is reflected in its mechanistic plurality. |
| OBS-060-T2-110 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Yes — suffering is a pervasive context for the characteristic across three groups. (1) As the trigger for compassionate giving (872-002): Leah's suffering (being hated) triggers divine giving (Gen 29:33, OBS-060-037). Hosea's Israel suffering from lack, drought, and need is the context of giving in the broader 872-002 corpus. (2) As the context within which human self-giving is enacted (872-005): the Servant gives his back to those who strike — bodily suffering is the medium of the self-giving (Isa 50:6, OBS-060-045). Job's devastating losses are the context of his blessing-response (Job 1:21, OBS-060-044). (3) As the condition that eschatological giving addresses (872-007): "those who mourn in Zion" — mourning/grief is the before-state that the eschatological giving transforms (Isa 61:3, OBS-060-048). The characteristic operates as: response to suffering (compassionate mode), act within suffering (self-giving mode), and remedy for suffering (eschatological mode). |
| OBS-060-T2-111 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Suffering reveals and tests the characteristic most clearly. Job 1:21 is paradigmatic: the loss of everything reveals whether inner relinquishment and worship-under-loss is genuine. The suffering does not produce the characteristic — it exposes whether it is present. The Servant's self-giving (Isa 50:6) similarly: the suffering reveals the depth of covenantal trust and inner commitment. The suffering does not generate the trust — but it is the context in which trust's reality is proven. In the eschatological mode: suffering (mourning, faint spirit) is the before-state that the divine giving addresses — in this case suffering is neither the producer nor the test but the condition that occasions the transformative giving. The characteristic is therefore suffering-tested (revealed by suffering) in the human direction and suffering-responsive (triggered by suffering) in the divine direction. |
| OBS-060-T2-112 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Yes — primarily through the endowing mode (872-003). The Spirit given to instruct (Neh 9:20) is a formative gift: its purpose is to shape the inner-cognitive-moral life over time, equipping the person for faithful walk. The wisdom given to the inward parts (Job 38:36) constitutes the deep cognitive capacity from which discerning character grows. The covenantal giving (872-001) participates in the formation of covenantal identity across generations — the Nehemiah corpus (Neh 9) is explicitly a rehearsal of divine giving as the ground of renewed covenantal commitment: the memory of God's giving shapes the present community's character orientation. The eschatological transformation ("oaks of righteousness," Isa 61:3) names the endpoint of the formation arc — the person shaped by divine giving reaches stable moral identity. |
| OBS-060-T2-113 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | The characteristic plays two roles in the formation arc. (1) Constitutive: the endowing giving deposits the capacities (Spirit, wisdom, understanding) that make formation possible. Formation cannot proceed without the constitutive inner-being endowment — this is the starting gift. (2) Sustaining: the covenantal giving across generations sustains the community's formation context — the memory of divine faithfulness is the sustained environment within which individual and communal character is shaped. The Neh 9 corpus illustrates this: Israel's formation is narrated as the history of divine giving (bread, water, Spirit, law, land) — the long arc of formation is the long arc of received giving. The giving is not incidental to formation; it is the ongoing substance of it. The person shaped by received giving is the person who has been formed by the pattern of divine faithfulness over time. |
| OBS-060-T2-114 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | Yes — group 872-007 is entirely eschatological in orientation and confirmed this at T0.2.3 (Q&A-006). Isa 61:3 names the eschatological endpoint: "oaks of righteousness, the planting of the Lord, that he may be glorified" — a stable, communal, identity-conferring state of righteousness that belongs to the age of divine restoration. Hos 2:15 invokes the Exodus as a type of the eschatological restoration — the future fullness is modelled on and exceeds the foundational covenant-giving moment. The present experience of covenantal giving (872-001) is itself oriented toward this fullness: the land given to Abraham is the earthly figuration of the eschatological inheritance. Isa 61:8 ("I will faithfully give them their recompense, and I will make an everlasting covenant with them") names the eschatological covenantal giving as the fullness toward which present covenantal giving points. |
| OBS-060-T2-115 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Isa 61:3 | The present experience of covenantal giving anticipates: (1) Completeness — every partial giving (land, provision, endowment) is oriented toward the fullness of the eschatological inheritance where the giving is total and permanent. The "everlasting covenant" and "everlasting name" of the Isaian texts name a giving that does not reserve or withhold. (2) Inner-being fullness — the eschatological giving transforms the inner person completely: faint spirit to praise, mourning to gladness, ashes to beauty. The present partial experience of inner renewal (Spirit-giving for instruction, compassionate provision for need) anticipates a state where no inner-being faculty remains in depletion. (3) Communal identity — "oaks of righteousness... the planting of the Lord" is communal: the present individual experiences of receiving divine giving anticipate a communal eschatological state of shared righteous identity. The present experience is partial, individual, and provisional; the future fullness is total, communal, and permanent. |
| OBS-060-T2-117 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Isa 61:3 | Using the G.2 co-occurrence data (OBS-060-029, OBS-060-030): the strongest adjacent characteristics by shared verse count are R099 kindness (12 shared verses), R183 heart (12), R180 yielding (11), R204 name (11), R197 authority (9), R187 strength (8), R019 calling (7), R034 covenant (7). The anchor verse analysis confirms four of these as analytically significant: R034 covenant (directly present in groups 872-001 and 872-004 through the covenant structure), R183 heart (leb in Hos 11:8 and Neh 9:8), R099 kindness/hesed (structural overlap with compassionate giving), R121 praise (shared anchor Isa 61:3 — five shared anchor registries). Additionally R023 compassion (shared anchor Hos 11:8), R103 love (shared anchor Jer 12:7), R194 blessing and R206 vulnerability (shared anchor Job 1:21). |
| OBS-060-T2-119 | VERSE_PATTERN | Isa 61:3 | Two sequential patterns are evidenced across the anchor verses. (1) Divine giving → human worship: Gen 12:7 (giving → altar-building), Hos 2:15 (giving → "she shall answer"), Isa 61:3 (giving → praise-garment). The characteristic consistently precedes worship/praise in these sequences. (2) Covenant faithfulness → covenant violation → judicial giving-over: the 872-001 → 872-004 sequence is a narrative pattern across the Neh 9, Hosea, and Jeremiah corpora. Faithfulness-as-giving (872-001) precedes faithlessness-response-giving (872-004) in the covenantal history. (3) Inner suffering → divine hearing → compassionate giving: Gen 29:33 shows this three-step sequence (Leah's suffering heard → child given). These are consistent enough across the corpus to constitute reliable sequential patterns. |
| OBS-060-T2-121 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Isa 61:3 | Three produced characteristics are evidenced. (1) Worship/praise (R121, R176): divine covenantal giving produces worship (Gen 12:7 — altar-building; Isa 61:3 — garment of praise; Job 1:21 — "blessed be the name of the Lord"). The mechanism is responsive: received giving evokes worship as the proper inner-being response. (2) Hope (R078): eschatological giving produces hope — Hos 2:15 transforms the Valley of Achor into a door of hope. The mechanism is transformative: the act of giving replaces shame with hope. (3) Understanding/wisdom: endowing giving (Neh 9:20, Job 38:36) produces cognitive capacity — the mechanism is constitutive, depositing what was not there. Each produced characteristic is produced by a distinct mode: responsive → worship; transformative → hope; constitutive → wisdom. |
| OBS-060-T2-130 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Isa 61:3 | Yes — 12 shared anchor verses confirmed in G.3 (OBS-060-031, OBS-060-034 through OBS-060-038). Key shared anchors: Isa 61:3 (R006 anointing, R071 grief, R121 praise, R151 sorrow), Hos 11:8 (R023 compassion, R120 perverseness, R151 sorrow), Job 1:21 (R194 blessing, R206 vulnerability), Jer 12:7 (R103 love), Gen 12:7 (R058 experience), Job 38:36 (R183 heart). These shared anchors are the most cross-registry dense verses in the registry. Isa 61:3 anchors five other registries alongside R060 — the highest density. |
| OBS-060-T2-131 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Isa 61:3 | Each shared anchor reveals a specific cross-registry relationship. (1) Isa 61:3 shared with R006, R071, R121, R151: the transformative-giving text simultaneously engages anointing, grief, praise, and sorrow — this verse is a structural node where multiple characteristics converge. Giving is the act that binds them: the anointing, the release from grief, the gift of praise, the end of sorrow are all forms of divine giving in the same verse. (2) Hos 11:8 shared with R023, R120, R151: compassion, perverseness, and sorrow are all present in the divine restraint-from-giving-over verse — the giving is withheld because of compassion, and is provoked by perverseness, in the context of grief. These three characteristics define the relational situation that determines the giving. (3) Job 1:21 shared with R194, R206: blessing and vulnerability are both products of the same experience of divine giving-and-taking — the verse holds them together. (4) Jer 12:7 shared with R103: love and judicial giving are structurally fused — the giving-over proceeds from love. Each shared anchor reveals a genuine inter-characteristic relationship, not coincidence. |
| OBS-060-T2-132 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Isa 61:3 | R060 carries five confirmed dimensions (T1.8): Moral Character (primary), Relational Disposition, Cognition, Transformation, Divine-Human Correspondence. All five are programme dimensions shared by multiple other registries. The most significant overlaps by co-occurrence signal: (1) Moral Character — shared with R067 (goodness), R090 (innocence), R015 (boastfulness — its opposite), R034 (covenant). (2) Relational Disposition — shared with R099 (kindness/hesed), R103 (love), R023 (compassion), R111 (mercy). (5) DHC — shared with R176 (worship), R121 (praise), which co-occur at 6 and 5 shared verses respectively. All five dimensions show confirmed co-occurrence overlap with other registries. |
| OBS-060-T2-143 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | Isa 61:3 | The anchor verse corpus spans four literary forms. (1) Narrative (Gen 12:7, Gen 15:18, Gen 29:33, Job 1:21): responsible interpretation requires attention to plot and character — the giving is embedded in a specific relational and historical moment. (2) Prophecy (Isa 61:3, Isa 50:6, Hos 11:8, Hos 13:11, Jer 12:7, Jer 13:16): responsible interpretation requires attention to rhetorical address and the distinction between divine speech and prophetic interpretation. The first-person divine speeches ("I gave," "How can I give you up") require particular care about the nature of divine inner-being language. (3) Wisdom/divine speech (Job 38:36, Job 1:21): responsible interpretation requires attention to the dramatic frame — Job's blessing is within a frame where Satan has made a claim, and God's question from the whirlwind is within a legal disputation. (4) Liturgical/prayer (Neh 9:20): responsible interpretation requires attention to the retrospective narrative form — this is communal confession of divine faithfulness, not direct reportage. The diversity of literary forms means no single interpretive lens covers the whole corpus. |
| OBS-060-T2-145 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | Isa 61:3 | The anchor verses are distributed across four contextual settings. (1) Covenantal: Gen 12:7, Gen 15:18, Mal 2:5, Neh 9:8 — formal covenant-establishment or covenantal renewal contexts. These settings require the giving to be read as covenantally binding, not merely generous. (2) Prophetic-judicial: Jer 12:7, Hos 13:11, Isa 42:8, Isa 47:6 — prophetic contexts of divine judgement and address to Israel. These require the giving to be read as morally consistent with the divine character, not arbitrary. (3) Eschatological-prophetic: Isa 61:3, Hos 2:15, Isa 35:2 — contexts of divine restoration announcement. These require the giving to be read as new-creation initiative, not mere restoration to prior state. (4) Narrative-individual: Gen 29:33, Job 1:21, Job 38:36 — individual persons encountering divine giving. These require attention to the specific relational situation of the individual. The diversity of settings confirms that inner-being giving is not a specialised sub-category of divine action but a pervasive mode present across the full range of biblical contexts. |
| OBS-060-T2-146 | VERSE_ANNOTATION | Isa 61:3 | Two candidates present themselves for different reasons, and the choice depends on which dimension of the characteristic is primary. (1) Hos 11:8 — the most comprehensive: it holds the judicial giving-over and the compassionate withholding in tension, names the divine inner-being faculties (leb, rahamim) explicitly, shows the inner-being movement that determines giving, and reveals the structural relationship between justice and compassion in divine faithfulness. No other verse holds this much of the characteristic's architecture simultaneously. (2) Isa 61:3 — the most eschatologically complete: it shows the transformative mode at full expression, names the spirit faculty directly, holds mourning and praise in one verse, and points toward the endpoint of the formation arc. Both verses are programme-density nodes (shared with 3-5 other registries). Assessment: Hos 11:8 is the primary analytical anchor — it reveals the inner-being decision-structure of divine giving most directly. Isa 61:3 is the primary transformative anchor. |
| OBS-060-T2-148 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Two frameworks are relevant at different levels. (1) Moral philosophy (virtue ethics): the characteristic as analysed engages moral character (the primary dimension), voluntary action, the relationship between inner disposition and outer act, and the formation of stable character over time — all central concerns of virtue ethics (Aristotle, MacIntyre). Virtue ethics provides a framework for understanding how faithfulness-as-giving constitutes character rather than merely performing actions. (2) Attachment theory (developmental psychology/relational psychology): the responsive-giving mode (872-002) and the eschatological-restoration mode (872-007) engage attachment dynamics — attentiveness to the inner condition of the attached person, the protective withholding of harm (Hos 11:8), the restoration of relational responsiveness (Hos 2:15). Attachment theory illuminates the relational-dispositional dimension of the characteristic. Moral philosophy is the most useful for the overall characteristic; attachment theory is most useful for the compassionate-responsive mode. |
| OBS-060-T2-149 | OBSERVATION | Isa 61:3 | Virtue ethics illuminates the disposition-in-action structure (Q&A-016): in virtue theory, a virtue is precisely a stable disposition that manifests in characteristic action — the virtuous person not only acts rightly but does so from a settled inner orientation. Na.tan-in-its-inner-being-register is the verb of virtuous action in this sense: the giving is virtuous when it proceeds from stable inner commitment (covenant faithfulness) rather than from compulsion or calculation. The framework also illuminates the formation arc (T5.5): virtue ethics explains how repeated patterns of giving-from-commitment form the stable character described as "oaks of righteousness" (Isa 61:3) — character is built through enacted disposition over time. Attachment theory illuminates Hos 11:8 coherently: the pattern of threatened loss of attachment → emotional response (heart recoiling, compassion warming) → protective action (withholding giving-over) matches the attachment theory account of how secure attachment operates in caregiving relationships. |

<a id="t-G1343"></a>
### G1343 dikaiosunē — righteousness (`mti_id=950`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `G1343` · Lang: Greek · Owner: OWNER
2. Registry: R098 justice · Legacy C-cluster: C13
3. Sub-group: `M26-A2` (Human Righteousness — State of being right) · `M26-G` (Structural Opposites: Wickedness and Injustice) · `M26-BOUNDARY` (BOUNDARY) · `M26-A1` (God Righteousness)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 353
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=950` · `md_version=1` · `vc_status=not_done`
6. Mounce short-def: righteousness, what is right, justice, the act of doing what is in agreement with God’s standards, the state of being in proper relationship with God

**Verses:** 74 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (16)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 2401 | 1225-001 | Acting wickedly — inner act of moral departure from God and righteousness |  |
| 1424 | 950-001 | Term names righteousness received through a divine crediting act — counted, reckoned, and conferred rather than earned or produced. The foundational OT instance is Abraham: he believed God, and it was counted to him as righteousness (Gen 15:6), the pattern Paul unfolds in Romans 4 (4:3, 4:5, 4:9, 4:11, 4:13, 4:22). The crediting pattern extends beyond faith alone: a specific act of justice toward the poor is credited as righteousness before the Lord (Deu 24:13); an act of zeal for God's honour is counted as righteousness from generation to generation forever (Psa 106:31). In the NT, the pattern reaches its definitive personal expression: not having a righteousness of my own that comes from the law, but that which comes through faith in Christ, the righteousness from God that depends on faith (Phili 3:9). Christ is the telos of the law for righteousness to everyone who believes (Rom 10:4). The faith-righteousness itself speaks and invites (Rom 10:6). Righteousness received through faith is the standing conferred, not attained — granted by God on the basis of trust, not performance. |  |
| 1425 | 950-002 | Term names righteousness as a practiced, lived orientation — the daily direction of a person's conduct before God. It is not a one-time standing but a manner of life expressed in what the person does with their whole self. The members of the body are presented as instruments of righteousness (Rom 6:13); the person becomes a slave of righteousness (Rom 6:18) — subjecting the whole self to righteousness as a governing master — and this slavery produces progressive sanctification (Rom 6:19). The lived orientation is more acceptable to God than ritual performance: to do righteousness and justice is more acceptable to the Lord than sacrifice (Pro 21:3). It is the pattern of daily conduct: the king who ate, drank, and did justice and righteousness — it was well with him (Jer 22:15). It is formable through scripture and discipline (2Ti 3:16, Heb 12:11) and can grow: Scripture produces training in righteousness, and discipline yields the peaceful fruit of righteousness to those who have been trained by it (Heb 12:11). This is righteousness in its daily, bodily, progressive expression. |  |
| 3627 | M26-A1-001 | Term declares God's righteousness as a foundational, permanent attribute of his nature — not situational, not responsive to circumstances, not capable of violation. It is affirmed across all his ways and all his works (Psa 145:17), endures forever (Psa 111:3), reaches to the high heavens (Psa 71:19), and will never be dismayed (Isa 51:6). It is the structural basis of his throne (Psa 89:14, 97:2), and paired consistently with faithfulness, holiness, and mercy. The closest the text comes to defining it is by exclusion: he does no injustice (Zep 3:5). |  |
| 3628 | M26-A1-002 | Term names God's righteousness as expressed through his judicial acts — judgments that conform to what is right rather than to preference or power. God judges the world with righteousness (Psa 9:8), his throne gives righteous judgment (Psa 9:4), the heavens declare it (Psa 50:6), and his judgments are named just in their most severe eschatological expressions (Rev 15:3–19:2). Christ's judgment is just because it aligns with the Father's will (Joh 5:30). God's righteous rules are expressions of this judicial character (Psa 119:7, 75, 106). The law is holy and righteous and good (Rom 7:12); the commandments of God are so righteous that no other nation has statutes and rules as righteous (Deu 4:8); his testimonies are righteous forever and every righteous rule endures (Psa 119:144, 160). His righteous judgment is grounded in complete knowledge: he is the one who knows the hearts of all (Act 1:24, 15:8) — the judicial righteousness is not formal but penetrating. Every transgression and disobedience received a just retribution (Heb 2:2); their condemnation is just (Rom 3:8). |  |
| 3630 | M26-A1-004 | Term names God's righteousness as the basis on which he acts to save, deliver, vindicate, and redeem — the quality that makes him reliably and confidently appealable. The psalmists invoke it repeatedly as the ground of petition: 'In your righteousness deliver me' (Psa 31:1, 71:2, 143:1, 143:11). God's saving acts are expressions of his righteousness (1Sa 12:7). Righteousness and salvation travel inseparably (Isa 45:8, 46:13, 51:5). He vindicates the righteous by rewarding him according to his righteousness (1Ki 8:32, 2Ch 6:23); he restores to man his righteousness (Job 33:26); he brings forth righteousness as the light and justice as the noonday (Psa 37:6); justice returns to the righteous (Psa 94:15). The prayer of appeal is heard: standing before God's house and crying out — God answers for his name's sake (2Ch 20:9). In the NT, God's righteousness is the basis on which he forgives (1Jo 1:9), and the ground of the cross — where he is simultaneously just and the justifier (Rom 3:26). God gives his righteousness to his people as gift and vindication (Psa 24:5, Isa 54:17). |  |
| 3631 | M26-A1-005 | Term names God's righteousness as something that must be declared, proclaimed, and made known — it is not self-evident but requires witness. The psalmist's tongue speaks of it all day (Psa 35:28, 71:24, 145:7), future generations shall proclaim it (Psa 22:31), the heavens declare it (Psa 50:6, 97:6). God's righteous word goes out and does not return (Isa 45:23). In the NT it is revealed through the gospel (Rom 1:17), manifested apart from law (Rom 3:21), and its glory exceeds the ministry of condemnation (2Cor 3:9). The Spirit convicts the world concerning it (Joh 16:8). The proclamation of God's righteousness is a sustained act of the covenant community across all time. |  |
| 3632 | M26-A1-006 | Term names God's righteousness as the standard that exposes human unrighteousness by contrast — when God's righteousness is affirmed, human guilt and shame are simultaneously revealed. The pattern recurs throughout Scripture: 'you are righteous; we have acted wickedly' (Neh 9:33), 'you are just; we are before you in guilt' (Ezr 9:15), 'The Lord is in the right; I have rebelled against his word' (Lam 1:18), 'to you belongs righteousness, but to us open shame' (Dan 9:7). Human anger cannot produce God's righteousness (Jam 1:20); human self-established righteousness fails to submit to it (Rom 10:3). The standard does not remain abstract: when Paul reasoned about righteousness and self-control and the coming judgment, Felix was alarmed (Act 24:25) — the standard of God's righteousness invades the conscience of those who hear it and produces alarm even in those who resist it. |  |
| 3633 | M26-A1-007 | Term names righteousness as the defining quality of the coming Messianic figure — the Righteous One, the righteous Branch, the righteous Servant. The Servant's righteousness enables him to make many accounted righteous (Isa 53:11); the Branch is named 'The Lord is our righteousness' (Jer 23:6, 33:16); the Messianic king is 'righteous and having salvation' (Zec 9:9); Christ's love of righteousness grounds his anointing (Psa 45:7, Heb 1:9); he rides out victoriously for the cause of truth and meekness and righteousness (Psa 45:4). The prayer for the Messianic king's righteousness (Psa 72:2) is answered in Christ, and through the seventy weeks God brings in everlasting righteousness (Dan 9:24). Wisdom who walks in the paths of righteousness (Pro 8:20) and Melchizedek the king of righteousness (Heb 7:2) are types of this figure. In the NT, Jesus is identified as the Holy and Righteous One (Act 3:14), whose coming was betrayed (Act 7:52), who was appointed to be seen by his servant (Act 22:14). Even the Roman governor testifies to his innocence three times — I find no guilt in him (Joh 18:38, 19:4, 19:6; Luk 23:4, 14, 22; Act 13:28) — the pagan authority bears triple witness to Messianic righteousness. He died 'the righteous for the unrighteous' (1Pe 3:18), is called as advocate 'Jesus Christ the righteous' (1Jo 2:1), and as the source of eternal salvation to all who obey him (Heb 5:9). He is the telos of the law for righteousness (Rom 10:4). God's righteousness does not remain external — it becomes incarnate in the Messiah and through him is extended to all who believe. |  |
| 3610 | M26-A2-001 | Term names righteousness as a governing quality whose presence produces measurable outcomes — deliverance from death, stability of the way, sure reward, honour, enduring legacy, and ultimately peace, quietness, and trust (Isa 32:17). Righteousness is not described here as a disposition to be cultivated but as a quality already operative whose consequences are stated. Its outcomes extend from the immediate (life over death, house standing, city rejoicing) to the eschatological (crown of righteousness awarded by the righteous judge, 2Ti 4:8). Contrasted consistently with wickedness whose outcomes are death and captivity. |  |
| 3612 | M26-A2-003 | Term names the failure, distortion, or counterfeit form of righteousness across a wide range of expressions: righteous deeds that are polluted and cannot stand before God (Isa 64:6); swearing in God's name but without righteousness (Isa 48:1); outward seeking without inner reality (Isa 58:2); righteousness claimed as the basis for divine favour but three times explicitly denied (Deu 9:4, 5, 6); righteousness perverted at national scale — justice turned to wormwood, the fruit of righteousness turned to poison (Amo 5:7, 6:12); wickedness occupying the institutional place assigned to righteousness (Ecc 3:16, 5:8); building and ruling by unrighteousness (Jer 22:13); law-righteousness pursued but never reached (Rom 9:31); counterfeit servants disguising themselves as servants of righteousness (2Cor 11:15); self-assessed righteousness producing contempt (Luk 18:9); outward appearance concealing inner lawlessness (Mat 23:28). Together these verses define what genuine righteousness is by documenting everything it is not. |  |
| 3615 | M26-A2-006 | Term names righteousness as the object of active inner longing and deliberate pursuit — hungered for, thirsted for, fled toward (Mat 5:6, 1Ti 6:11, 2Ti 2:22). The righteous person is defined by the direction of inner desire. The degree of righteousness matters (exceeding the scribes and Pharisees, Mat 5:20). Pursuit is commanded, implying the state is not yet fully arrived at but is the defining goal of the inner person. Beyond pursuit, righteousness is also a skill that must be developed: the person who lives on milk is unskilled in the word of righteousness (Heb 5:13), implying that maturity and practice produce competence in righteousness. The state is sought, and it is also formed. |  |
| 3616 | M26-A2-007 | Term names righteousness as a generative quality — something sown, grown, harvested, and multiplied. The agricultural image is most precise: sow for yourselves righteousness and reap steadfast love (Hos 10:12) — righteousness as seed produces a different and greater harvest. The fruit of light is found in all that is good and right and true (Eph 5:9) — righteousness is what light produces in those who walk in it. The fruit of righteousness comes through Jesus Christ, to the glory and praise of God (Phili 1:11), and the harvest of righteousness increases when God multiplies the sower's seed (2Cor 9:10). Righteousness is not static possession but active production. |  |
| 3617 | M26-A2-008 | Term names righteousness as the defining constitution of the new self in Christ. The new self is created after the likeness of God in true righteousness and holiness (Eph 4:24). Righteousness is worn as a breastplate — protective, constant, part of the person's standing equipment (Eph 6:14). Most radically: God made Christ to be sin so that in him we might become the righteousness of God (2Cor 5:21) — the person is not merely covered by righteousness or credited with it, but constituted as the righteousness of God in Christ. These verses describe righteousness not as something to be pursued but as something already constitutive of the new identity in Christ, reaching its fullest expression in the identification of the person with God's own righteous character. |  |
| 3623 | M26-A2-014 | Term names righteousness as a state defined by and constituted through its orientation toward God. The righteous person walks with God (Gen 6:9), lives by faith (Hab 2:4), serves God as the defining mark of their identity (Mal 3:18), keeps God's statutes faithfully (Eze 18:9), and brings offerings in righteousness (Mal 3:3). Righteousness qualifies the person to enter through the gates of the Lord (Psa 118:20) and to enter the gates of righteousness to give thanks (Psa 118:19). The covenant oath is sworn in truth, justice, and righteousness (Jer 4:2). Human righteousness is done in active response to God's coming righteousness: keep justice and do righteousness, for my salvation is near (Isa 56:1). Righteousness flows from God through the new birth: everyone who practices righteousness has been born of him (1Jo 2:29); whoever practices righteousness is righteous, as he is righteous (1Jo 3:7) — the person's righteousness corresponds to and flows from God's own. The kingdom of God is righteousness and peace and joy in the Holy Spirit (Rom 14:17). Whether it is right in the sight of God to listen to you rather than to God — the God-frame is the ultimate test of what is right (Act 4:19). |  |
| 3625 | M26-A2-016 | Term names the righteous person as a specific target of hostility — plotted against (Psa 37:12), watched for death (Psa 37:32), spoken against with contempt (Psa 31:18), sold for silver (Amo 2:6), afflicted by corrupt leaders (Amo 5:12), deprived of justice by bribes (Isa 5:23, Pro 17:15), and killed (Lam 4:13, 2Sa 4:11). The opposition is directed precisely because the person is righteous: the wicked surround the righteous so that justice goes forth perverted (Hab 1:4); even false prophecy is used to dishearten the righteous (Eze 13:22). The pattern is named and blessed by Jesus: Blessed are those who are persecuted for righteousness' sake, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven (Mat 5:10). The opposition is not incidental but structural — righteousness provokes hostility from those whose character is defined against it. The enemy of the righteous person is characterised as an enemy of all righteousness (Act 13:10): the opposition is not personal but comprehensive. |  |

**Findings — direct (Strong's mention) (1)**

| Finding ID | Type | Anchor verses | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| DIM-98-002 | DIMENSION_REVIEW |  | The dikaios/dikaiosunē/dikaiōsis/dikaioō family (G1342, G1343, G1344, G1347) shows a consistent tension: righteousness as divine act/attribute (Theological/Divine-Human) vs righteousness as human inner quality (Moral/Conscience). This is one of the most analytically significant tensions in the programme. Session B must examine this carefully. |
