| DIM-160-004 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | Rom 4:9 | Registry 160 (thought) contains three instances of the directionally-determined inner faculty pattern: (1) re.a (H7454) — 3453-001 names thoughts known by God and God's thoughts precious to the psalmist, the same relational faculty operating from both sides of the divine-human boundary simultaneously (Sub-pattern C); (2) dialogismos (G1261) — 3409-001 names inner thoughts as site of spiritual warfare and accusation (Moral/Conscience) and 3410-001 names inner reckoning as the ground of faith (Cognitive/Mind) — same root, opposite orientation; (3) fronimos (G5429) pattern carries from C01: prudence and craftiness as the same perceptive faculty (Sub-pattern B). Session B must treat these as analytically distinct uses of the same terms. |
| DIM-97-003 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | Rom 14:17 | Group 378-001 (chara — abiding inner state) is assigned Character/Disposition rather than Affective/Emotional because it names a stable characterising inner quality. This parallels gratitude in Registry 69. Both registries produce at least one group where the inner state is stable and dispositional rather than episodic. Session B should examine whether there is a category of joy-as-character-quality distinct from joy-as-affective-experience. |
| DIM-34-001 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | Eph 4:24 | Three groups within the covenant registry show the new covenant as inner transformation (765-003, 766-002, 777-001 — law written on heart, new covenant through Spirit, new self/creation in Christ). Session B should examine the covenant registry's inner transformation dimension: how the trajectory from the Sinai covenant (obedience required externally, inner-being orientation demanded) to the new covenant (transformation from within, law on heart) is encoded in the Dimension Review data and what this discloses about the inner person as the site of covenantal fulfilment. |
| 062-RELAT-001 | RELATIONAL_POSITIONING | 2Cor 6:14 | Fellowship fundamentally transforms relational positioning by creating horizontal fellowship chains grounded in vertical fellowship, establishing mutual support systems, creating moral alignment with righteousness or wickedness, potentially violating existing covenantal bonds through faithlessness, and requiring moral/spiritual compatibility where righteousness cannot fellowship with lawlessness. |
| 062-EXPR-001 | RELATIONAL_EXPRESSION | 2Cor 6:14 | Fellowship expresses relationally through proclaiming to create fellowship chains, mutual support in falling and restoration, moral alignment choice between righteous and wicked companionship, generous participation and shared commitment among believers, and avoiding incompatible fellowships. |

The ground is moral and spiritual alignment rather than preference or proximity. |
| OBS-103-T2-028 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Jam 2:23 | From the data: (a) **obedience** — 1Pe 1:22 (OBS-103-124): soul-purification through obedience enables sincere love. The purified soul is the condition for authentic love. (b) **faith** — Jam 2:23 (OBS-103-053): Abraham's faith is what constitutes him as 'friend of God.' Faith as the inner condition through which love-relationship with God becomes possible. (c) **openness / not loving the darkness** — Joh 3:19 (OBS-103-147): those who love darkness cannot receive the light. The inner orientation away from evil is the negative condition for receiving love. (d) **being the returning prodigal** — Luk 15:20 (OBS-103-057): the son's return (movement toward the father) is the condition for the father's embrace. Some movement toward the source of love appears to be a condition for its reception. Gap: the data does not contain an explicit systematic account of reception-conditions. The conditions above are inferred from contexts where love is given and received. The gap is noted. |
| OBS-111-T2-005 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 3:25 | The propitiation vocabulary points forward: Rom 3:25 (*hilastērios* received by faith) and the typological fulfillment of the mercy seat in Christ (OBS-111-066, OBS-111-067) establish mercy's eschatological trajectory through Christ. The Beatitude in Mat 5:7 ("they shall receive mercy") carries a future-tense promise that points toward the completion of mercy's logic. Heb 8:12's new covenant citation ("I will remember their sins no more") is eschatological in character — it names the final state of divine-human relationship under mercy. The evidence does not name a specific future fullness of mercy beyond these. OBS-111-032, OBS-111-035, OBS-111-066. |
| OBS-111-T2-009 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 3:25 | Yes — mercy has explicit typological architecture. The *kapporet* (mercy seat, H3727) is the physical OT locus of divine-human meeting in mercy; *hilastērios* (G2435) names Christ as the fulfillment of the mercy seat (Rom 3:25). The typological movement is explicit: physical mercy seat → Christ as living mercy seat received by faith (OBS-111-066, OBS-111-067). The Day of Atonement (*kippurim*, H3725) similarly finds typological fulfillment in Christ's once-for-all sacrifice (Heb 9–10, though the Heb 9:5 verse notes this trajectory). OBS-111-045, OBS-111-066, OBS-111-067. |
| OBS-111-T2-010 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 3:25 | The typological direction is human institution → divine person. The mercy seat as physical object (Heb 9:5) was the divine-established institution within Israel's worship; it points forward to Christ as the person in whom all that the mercy seat represented is fulfilled (Rom 3:25). The direction is not human → divine analogy (as if the human institution generates the divine reality) but divine establishment of the type followed by divine fulfillment in Christ. OBS-111-066, OBS-111-067. |
| OBS-111-T2-110 | OBSERVATION | Rom 3:25 | Yes — mechanism varies by context: OT ritual context uses blood-sacrifice; new covenant context uses faith-reception of the living mercy seat (Rom 3:25 — OBS-111-066); prayer context uses petition-response; Christ's priestly context uses incarnational solidarity (Heb 2:17). The mechanisms are contextually differentiated but share a common logic: the mercy mechanism is always specific, embodied, and costs something (blood, incarnation, divine speech, answered petition). OBS-111-033, OBS-111-037, OBS-111-060, OBS-111-066. |
| OBS-111-T2-115 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 3:25 | Yes — three eschatological pointers: (1) Heb 8:12 (new covenant mercy — "I will remember their sins no more") is the eschatological state of the divine-human relationship as mercy-constituted. (2) Mat 5:7 ("they shall receive mercy") points forward — the promise is future-tense. (3) The typological fulfillment structure (mercy seat → Christ as living mercy seat, OBS-111-066) points to the completed mercy-reality in Christ that the OT types anticipated. OBS-111-032, OBS-111-035, OBS-111-066. |
| OBS-062-T2-165 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | Fellowship explicitly excludes the claim that mere proximity constitutes participation — the English "fellowship" risks this, but the vocabulary insists on genuine sharing-in. 2Cor 6:14 ("what fellowship has light with darkness?") draws the line explicitly: apparent co-presence does not constitute fellowship; shared nature is required. OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-062-T2-189 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | 2Cor 6:14 | The mind as a distinct faculty is not explicitly named in the fellowship anchor verses. Cognitive assessment of the fellowship object is implied — 2Cor 6:14 ("what fellowship has light with darkness?") is a cognitive-evaluative question — but the mind is not named as the locus of fellowship itself. OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-062-T2-206 | OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | Yes — perception is engaged in the discernment of fellowship objects and their nature. 2Cor 6:14 ("what fellowship has light with darkness?") is a perceptive question — the question itself presupposes that the fellowship-seeker can perceive the nature of what they are joining with. 2Cor 6:14's implicit answer is that light and darkness are perceptively distinguishable, and fellowship-seeking requires this distinction. OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-062-T2-207 | OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | Fellowship enables perception in two directions: it enables accurate perception of the fellowship object's nature (2Cor 6:14 — the call to discern light from darkness), and inverted fellowship impairs perception — Hos 4:17 presents Ephraim "joined to idols" as a condition in which the joining itself has disabled exit. Habitual wrong-fellowship may progressively impair the perception needed to recognise the error. OBS-062-042, OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-062-T2-209 | OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | Cognition is engaged in the choice and evaluation of fellowship objects. Psa 119:63 is a cognitively deliberate alignment — the psalmist has assessed who fears God and aligned with them. 2Cor 6:14 is a cognitive incompatibility argument. OBS-062-048, OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-062-T2-224 | OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | Yes — deeply. Fellowship with God-fearers produces righteous character (Psa 119:63); fellowship with thieves produces corrupt character (Isa 1:23). Moral complicity (Mat 23:30) is explicitly a moral-evaluative fellowship act. 2Cor 6:14 poses the incompatibility of light-and-darkness fellowship as a moral-evaluative question. OBS-062-048, OBS-062-049, OBS-062-057, OBS-062-059. |
| OBS-062-T2-227 | OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | Yes — conscience is engaged in the moral complicity mode. Mat 23:30 ("we would not have taken part with them in shedding the blood of the prophets") is a conscience-inflected statement — the attempt to dissociate from inherited moral complicity is a conscience response. 2Cor 6:14's incompatibility argument implicitly invokes conscience as the faculty that recognises the wrongness of light-darkness fellowship. OBS-062-059, OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-062-T2-290 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | 2Cor 6:14 | The structural opposite/absence appears in the verse evidence (Ecc 4:10 — "woe to him who is alone"; 2Cor 6:14 — incompatibility of light/darkness fellowship) but not in a dedicated lexical form. The absence of fellowship is described circumstantially, not named by a single antonym. OBS-062-050, OBS-062-049. |
| OBS-034-T2-111 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 4:24 | Yes — covenant is the structural framework within which the entire arc of character formation and sanctification takes place in Scripture. (1) The Sinai covenant's law shapes moral character over time through sustained discipline and obedience — the person who keeps covenant ("keeps his commandments" — Deu 7:9; "keeps his covenant and his testimonies" — Psa 25:10 — OBS-034-039) is progressively formed into a person of integrity. (2) The new covenant constitutionally initiates this formation at a deeper level: law written on the heart (Heb 8:10 — OBS-034-059) is the constitutional foundation for ongoing moral formation. The Spirit's administration (2 Cor 3:6 — OBS-034-061) produces what the law demanded. Heb 10:29 mentions "sanctified by the blood of the covenant" (OBS-034-070) — the covenant blood is the ground of sanctification, not merely justification. (3) The new self/new creation (777-002 — OBS-034-062) — kainos in the register of formation: Eph 4:24 — "put on the new self, created after the likeness of God in true righteousness and holiness." Covenant produces formation toward Godlikeness. |
| OBS-034-T2-112 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Eph 4:24 | Covenant plays a foundational, architecturally prior role in the arc of character formation and sanctification — it is not one element among equals but the structural framework that makes formation possible. Three specific roles: (1) Constitutional grounding — the new covenant's law-on-heart inscription (Heb 8:10) provides the inner constitutional ground from which all subsequent formation grows. Formation cannot proceed sustainably without this constitutional foundation. (2) Relational motivation — covenant positions the person in a relationship of known belonging ("I will be their God, they shall be my people" — Heb 8:10). Formation that flows from known belonging is qualitatively different from formation driven by fear of punishment. (3) Progressive sanctification through the blood — "sanctified by the blood of the covenant" (Heb 10:29 — OBS-034-070) — the covenant's atoning mechanism is the ongoing basis for the sanctification that formation requires. Without covenantal atonement, the moral weight of failure would prevent formation from proceeding. Covenant provides the structural conditions (constitution, belonging, atonement) within which formation operates. |
| OBS-117-T2-028 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — and this is theologically significant. The forming dimension: (1) Mal 2:6 — walking in peace as the character-across-time of the faithful priest; the formed person whose sustained walk is peaceful; (2) G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness as the fruit of discipline (Heb 12:11 — "the peaceful fruit of righteousness to those who have been trained by it"); (3) Gal 5:22 — peace as Spirit-fruit — the Spirit forms peace as a characteristic quality in the believer over time; (4) G2272 hēsuchios — the gentle and quiet spirit (1Pe 3:4) as the "hidden person of the heart" — a formed inner quality precious to God. The evidence confirms: sustained shalom is not merely experienced but formed — it becomes a character quality, an inner orientation, and eventually the "hidden person" of the heart. The transformative dimension connects directly to T5 (formative and developmental). |
| OBS-117-T2-096 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | The data implies that genuine peace-extension requires prior reception and formation: (1) Prior reception: the person who has not received peace cannot authentically give it — the greeting-blessing (1Sa 25:6) is an invocation of God's peace upon another, not a self-generated gift; the extension is mediated from the divine source; (2) Character formation: G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness as quality of heavenly wisdom and of discipline (Heb 12:11 — "the peaceful fruit of righteousness to those who have been trained by it") — the person who extends peace has been trained; (3) Identity-level commitment: Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace") — the giver of peace is a person whose identity is peace; the extension flows from being, not merely from choosing. The data does not require perfect peace in the giver — Jer 29:7 (exiles seeking the welfare of their captor city) shows peace-extension is possible even from a position of suffering — but it requires genuine orientation. |
| OBS-117-T2-097 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — but the data for human-from-human reception is less explicit than for God-from-human or human-to-human giving. Evidence: (1) The greeting-blessing: the recipients of the sha.lom blessing (1Sa 25:6's addressees) receive peace from the messenger; (2) The apostolic blessing-recipients: every Pauline letter recipient receives "grace and peace" in the greeting — the community receives peace through the apostolic proclamation; (3) G1516 — the discipline produces eirēnikos fruit in "those who have been trained by it" (Heb 12:11) — the person who submits to a trainer/community receives peace as a fruit of that relationship; (4) The woman healed in Luk 8:48 — "go in peace" — she receives peace from Christ's word; (5) Children of Zion taught by God: Isa 54:13 ("great shall be the peace of your children") — the covenant community's children receive peace through being taught. |
| OBS-117-T2-108 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — the data documents several sequences: (1) Lam 3 sequence: soul bereft of peace (3:17) → remembering God's hesed (3:21-22) → hope restored (3:24) → quiet waiting (3:26) — the sequence from peaceless despair through memory to hope through waiting; (2) Isa 30:15 sequence: returning → rest → quietness → trust → strength/salvation — a five-stage sequence where each stage enables the next; (3) Psa 32 sequence: sin → suppressed conscience → somatic deterioration → confession → divine forgiveness → peace; (4) Gift-to-fruit sequence: God gives peace (Num 6:26) → received in the mind/heart (Isa 26:3) → enacted in the body (Psa 4:8) → expressed in the community (Rom 12:18) — the reception-to-expression sequence; (5) Formation sequence: discipline → trained → peaceful fruit (Heb 12:11) — the formative sequence through suffering into peace. |
| OBS-117-T2-109 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | The sequences converge on three structural insights: (1) Peace is not arrived at directly but through precondition states — trust, returning, quietness, confession, discipline are all precondition stages that enable peace's reception; (2) Peace received produces enabling states — strength, hope, stability, and community expression flow from peace received; peace is both an end-state (received) and an enabling-state (productive); (3) The Lam 3 sequence is the most instructive for the peaceless person: the route from peacelessness back to peace is through memory of God's faithfulness (not through achieving peace directly). The sequence reveals that peace has a pathway structure — it is neither instantaneous (always) nor arbitrary in how it comes and goes. |
| OBS-117-T2-110 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | The data identifies multiple mechanisms: (1) Divine word/proclamation: Judg 6:23 ("Peace be to you") — the spoken word of peace from God or his messenger produces the inner shift from terror to peace; Joh 14:27 ("my peace I give to you") — Christ's word-gift; the proclamation is a mechanism; (2) Conscience-activation: Psa 32:3-5 — the mechanism is confession activating what suppressed conscience had blocked; the act of acknowledgment opens the peace-channel; (3) Cognitive re-direction: Isa 26:3 — the mechanism is the deliberate directing of the mind toward God; (4) Discipline and training: Heb 12:11 — the mechanism is the sustained experience of discipline by a trainer, producing peace as fruit over time; (5) Cross-accomplishment: Col 1:20 — the mechanism is Christ's blood of the cross making peace; a historical accomplished act; (6) Spirit indwelling: Gal 5:22 — the Spirit's presence produces peace as fruit through the mechanism of indwelling and progressive formation. |
| OBS-117-T2-111 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — the mechanisms differ by context: (1) Crisis/encounter context: divine word is the immediate mechanism (Judg 6:23, Dan 10:19, Joh 14:27) — peace comes through an encounter with divine presence or word; (2) Moral failure context: conscience-activation and confession are the mechanism (Psa 32:3-5) — the route back from peacelessness through sin is through honest acknowledgment; (3) Cognitive orientation context: sustained mind-direction is the mechanism (Isa 26:3, Rom 8:6) — for the ongoing inner condition of peace, the mechanism is daily cognitive orientation; (4) Formative context: discipline over time is the mechanism (Heb 12:11) — peace-as-character is formed through sustained training rather than instantaneous receipt; (5) Eschatological/cosmic context: Christ's cross is the mechanism (Col 1:20, Isa 53:5) — for the foundational peace-with-God and cosmic peace, the mechanism is unique historical accomplishment. No single mechanism covers all contexts. |
| OBS-117-T2-112 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — and the peace-suffering relationship is one of the most analytically significant in the entire registry. Evidence: (1) Isa 53:5 — "upon him was the chastisement that brought us peace, and with his wounds we are healed" — peace purchased through the Servant's suffering; (2) Isa 38:17 — "it was for my welfare that I had great bitterness" — Hezekiah's welfare arrived through his suffering; (3) Heb 12:11 — "for the moment all discipline seems painful rather than pleasant, but later it yields the peaceful fruit of righteousness" — peace is the outcome of endured discipline (suffering); (4) Lam 3:17 — "my soul is bereft of peace" — the peaceless condition in suffering; (5) 2Cor 2:13 — Paul's spirit not at rest amid his afflictions — the tension between suffering and peace; (6) 2Th 1:7 — "relief [anesis] to you who are afflicted... when the Lord Jesus is revealed" — eschatological peace as relief from present suffering. |
| OBS-117-T2-113 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | All four relationships appear: (1) Produces: Heb 12:11 — discipline produces peaceful fruit in those trained; Isa 38:17 — bitterness was the path to welfare; suffering is the generative mechanism in certain contexts; (2) Tests: Lam 3:17 — the soul bereft of peace is the person whose peace is tested to its limit; the sustained peacelessness under catastrophe tests whether the person's peace has deep roots or is situational; (3) Reveals: the person who maintains peace through suffering reveals a peace grounded in God rather than circumstance (Phili 4:7 — "surpasses all understanding" suggests it transcends circumstantial explanation); (4) Deepens: the retrospective memory of peace-through-suffering (Isa 38:17) deepens understanding of God's ways; suffering that produces peace creates a richer experiential knowledge of shalom than suffering-free peace. The paradox of T1 (Q&A-023) is confirmed here: suffering is not peace's enemy but its formative companion in the data. |
| OBS-117-T2-114 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — and this is explicitly evidenced: (1) 1Th 5:23 — "the God of peace himself sanctify you completely; your whole spirit and soul and body be kept blameless" — peace and sanctification are explicitly paired; the God of peace is the agent of complete sanctification; (2) Heb 12:11 — "the peaceful fruit of righteousness to those who have been trained" — peace is the outcome of the formative discipline that constitutes sanctification; (3) Gal 5:22 — Spirit-fruit peace — peace is a product of the Spirit's sanctifying work in the person over time; (4) Mal 2:6 — "he walked with me in peace and uprightness, and turned many from iniquity" — the long-arc portrait of the sanctified priest; peace is both the condition and the outcome of his sustained walk with God. |
| OBS-117-T2-115 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Peace plays a dual role in sanctification: (1) Condition for sanctification: 1Th 5:23 — the God of peace sanctifies; the peace-ground is the condition under which sanctification proceeds; (2) Fruit of sanctification: Gal 5:22 (Spirit-fruit), Heb 12:11 (fruit of discipline) — peace is what sanctification produces. This dual role (condition and fruit) makes peace both the environment of growth and its outcome — uniquely integrative. The implication: a person cannot be sanctified while persistently refusing peace (the refusal-pattern of Isa 30:15 is simultaneously a refusal of the conditions for sanctification); and when sanctification proceeds, peace is among its visible fruits. |
| OBS-117-T2-123 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 12:11 | Yes — documented: (1) Trust produces peace: Isa 26:3 (causal — "because he trusts in you"), Psa 4:8; (2) Righteousness produces peace: Isa 32:17 ("the effect of righteousness will be peace"); (3) Confession produces peace: Psa 32:5 (confession → forgiveness → peace); (4) Discipline produces peace: Heb 12:11 (training → peaceful fruit); (5) The Spirit produces peace: Gal 5:22 (Spirit-fruit); (6) Wisdom produces peace: Pro 3:17 ("all her paths are peace"); (7) Love of God's law produces peace: Psa 119:165 ("great peace have those who love your law"). The inputs to peace are numerous: trust, righteousness, wisdom, discipline, the Spirit, confession, love of God's law. This confirms that peace is not a simple direct gift but a multi-factored condition — multiple inner and relational realities contribute to its presence. |
| OBS-117-T2-154 | OBSERVATION | Heb 12:11 | Two gaps surfaced by the human science comparison: (1) Physiological regulation: the verse evidence shows somatic consequences of peacelessness (bones wasting, failing eyes, languishing soul) but does not address the physiological mechanisms connecting inner peace to somatic health — neuroscience of the stress response (cortisol, autonomic nervous system) would complement the biblical somatic evidence but is outside the verse data; (2) Developmental trajectory: attachment theory describes how secure attachment in early childhood shapes the capacity for adult peace and trust — the biblical data addresses formed peace in adults (Heb 12:11 — "those who have been trained") but does not address childhood formation of peace-capacity. Both gaps are relevant for Session C prose and for a comprehensive understanding of shalom's human experience, but they cannot be grounded in the biblical evidence directly. |

<a id="t-G1342"></a>
### G1342 dikaios — just (`mti_id=3193`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `G1342` · Lang: Greek · Owner: OWNER
2. Registry: R098 justice · Legacy C-cluster: C13
3. Sub-group: `M26-A2` (Human Righteousness — State of being right) · `M26-BOUNDARY` (BOUNDARY) · `M26-A1` (God Righteousness)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 383
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=3193` · `md_version=1` · `vc_status=not_done`
6. Mounce short-def: right, righteous, upright; in the NT this refers to God’s proper standards and actions, expressed in the covenants; as a noun it refers to a person in accord with God’s standards, in proper relationship with God just

**Verses:** 74 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (10)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1422 | 3193-001 | Term characterises the person as morally righteous — the inner quality of justice and uprightness that defines the person who stands rightly before God and others |  |
| 1423 | 3193-002 | Term names God and Christ as just and righteous — addressing the Father directly as 'righteous Father' (Joh 17:25), affirming God's judgments as just in their most severe expressions (Rev 15:3, 16:5, 16:7, 19:2). The VSG captures direct divine address and the eschatological declaration of God's justice as final judge. |  |
| 3627 | M26-A1-001 | Term declares God's righteousness as a foundational, permanent attribute of his nature — not situational, not responsive to circumstances, not capable of violation. It is affirmed across all his ways and all his works (Psa 145:17), endures forever (Psa 111:3), reaches to the high heavens (Psa 71:19), and will never be dismayed (Isa 51:6). It is the structural basis of his throne (Psa 89:14, 97:2), and paired consistently with faithfulness, holiness, and mercy. The closest the text comes to defining it is by exclusion: he does no injustice (Zep 3:5). |  |
| 3628 | M26-A1-002 | Term names God's righteousness as expressed through his judicial acts — judgments that conform to what is right rather than to preference or power. God judges the world with righteousness (Psa 9:8), his throne gives righteous judgment (Psa 9:4), the heavens declare it (Psa 50:6), and his judgments are named just in their most severe eschatological expressions (Rev 15:3–19:2). Christ's judgment is just because it aligns with the Father's will (Joh 5:30). God's righteous rules are expressions of this judicial character (Psa 119:7, 75, 106). The law is holy and righteous and good (Rom 7:12); the commandments of God are so righteous that no other nation has statutes and rules as righteous (Deu 4:8); his testimonies are righteous forever and every righteous rule endures (Psa 119:144, 160). His righteous judgment is grounded in complete knowledge: he is the one who knows the hearts of all (Act 1:24, 15:8) — the judicial righteousness is not formal but penetrating. Every transgression and disobedience received a just retribution (Heb 2:2); their condemnation is just (Rom 3:8). |  |
| 3630 | M26-A1-004 | Term names God's righteousness as the basis on which he acts to save, deliver, vindicate, and redeem — the quality that makes him reliably and confidently appealable. The psalmists invoke it repeatedly as the ground of petition: 'In your righteousness deliver me' (Psa 31:1, 71:2, 143:1, 143:11). God's saving acts are expressions of his righteousness (1Sa 12:7). Righteousness and salvation travel inseparably (Isa 45:8, 46:13, 51:5). He vindicates the righteous by rewarding him according to his righteousness (1Ki 8:32, 2Ch 6:23); he restores to man his righteousness (Job 33:26); he brings forth righteousness as the light and justice as the noonday (Psa 37:6); justice returns to the righteous (Psa 94:15). The prayer of appeal is heard: standing before God's house and crying out — God answers for his name's sake (2Ch 20:9). In the NT, God's righteousness is the basis on which he forgives (1Jo 1:9), and the ground of the cross — where he is simultaneously just and the justifier (Rom 3:26). God gives his righteousness to his people as gift and vindication (Psa 24:5, Isa 54:17). |  |
| 3633 | M26-A1-007 | Term names righteousness as the defining quality of the coming Messianic figure — the Righteous One, the righteous Branch, the righteous Servant. The Servant's righteousness enables him to make many accounted righteous (Isa 53:11); the Branch is named 'The Lord is our righteousness' (Jer 23:6, 33:16); the Messianic king is 'righteous and having salvation' (Zec 9:9); Christ's love of righteousness grounds his anointing (Psa 45:7, Heb 1:9); he rides out victoriously for the cause of truth and meekness and righteousness (Psa 45:4). The prayer for the Messianic king's righteousness (Psa 72:2) is answered in Christ, and through the seventy weeks God brings in everlasting righteousness (Dan 9:24). Wisdom who walks in the paths of righteousness (Pro 8:20) and Melchizedek the king of righteousness (Heb 7:2) are types of this figure. In the NT, Jesus is identified as the Holy and Righteous One (Act 3:14), whose coming was betrayed (Act 7:52), who was appointed to be seen by his servant (Act 22:14). Even the Roman governor testifies to his innocence three times — I find no guilt in him (Joh 18:38, 19:4, 19:6; Luk 23:4, 14, 22; Act 13:28) — the pagan authority bears triple witness to Messianic righteousness. He died 'the righteous for the unrighteous' (1Pe 3:18), is called as advocate 'Jesus Christ the righteous' (1Jo 2:1), and as the source of eternal salvation to all who obey him (Heb 5:9). He is the telos of the law for righteousness (Rom 10:4). God's righteousness does not remain external — it becomes incarnate in the Messiah and through him is extended to all who believe. |  |
| 3615 | M26-A2-006 | Term names righteousness as the object of active inner longing and deliberate pursuit — hungered for, thirsted for, fled toward (Mat 5:6, 1Ti 6:11, 2Ti 2:22). The righteous person is defined by the direction of inner desire. The degree of righteousness matters (exceeding the scribes and Pharisees, Mat 5:20). Pursuit is commanded, implying the state is not yet fully arrived at but is the defining goal of the inner person. Beyond pursuit, righteousness is also a skill that must be developed: the person who lives on milk is unskilled in the word of righteousness (Heb 5:13), implying that maturity and practice produce competence in righteousness. The state is sought, and it is also formed. |  |
| 3620 | M26-A2-011 | Term names righteousness as a governing inner orientation expressed through speech and discernment. The interior dimension is primary: the heart ponders how to answer (Pro 15:28); inner judgment of what is right precedes and governs speech (Luk 12:57; Joh 7:24 — do not judge by appearances but with right judgment). From this inner orientation, the mouth of the righteous produces life and nourishment (Pro 10:11, 10:21), the tongue speaks choice silver and wisdom (Pro 10:20, 10:31), the lips know what is acceptable (Pro 10:32). All the words of wisdom's mouth are righteous; there is nothing twisted or crooked in them (Pro 8:8). Truthful speech gives honest evidence (Pro 12:17); righteous lips are the delight of the king (Pro 16:13). Falsehood is hated as an inner aversion (Pro 13:5). The inner person is commanded to fill the mind with whatever is true, honourable, just, and pure (Phili 4:8) — establishing the cognitive foundation from which righteous speech flows. |  |
| 3621 | M26-A2-012 | Term names righteousness as an inner orientation that extends outward toward others — in all relationships and at every scale. It reaches the individual: the righteous person guides the neighbour (Pro 12:26), gives generously without withholding (Pro 21:26, Psa 37:21), cares even for animals (Pro 12:10), returns good for evil (1Sa 24:17), and deals fairly toward workers — whatever is right I will give you (Mat 20:4). It reaches the family: children obey parents because this is right (Eph 6:1); the apostle feels rightly toward the community (Phili 1:7). It reaches the vulnerable: do justice and righteousness and deliver from the hand of the oppressor (Jer 22:3); the king who daily ate, drank, and did justice and righteousness — it was well with him (Jer 22:15). At its fullest expression, righteousness rolls down like waters and flows as an ever-flowing stream (Amo 5:24) — not contained in isolated acts but pervading all relationships continuously. The righteous person's inner state does not rest in itself but reaches toward the needs of others at every level of human life. |  |
| 3623 | M26-A2-014 | Term names righteousness as a state defined by and constituted through its orientation toward God. The righteous person walks with God (Gen 6:9), lives by faith (Hab 2:4), serves God as the defining mark of their identity (Mal 3:18), keeps God's statutes faithfully (Eze 18:9), and brings offerings in righteousness (Mal 3:3). Righteousness qualifies the person to enter through the gates of the Lord (Psa 118:20) and to enter the gates of righteousness to give thanks (Psa 118:19). The covenant oath is sworn in truth, justice, and righteousness (Jer 4:2). Human righteousness is done in active response to God's coming righteousness: keep justice and do righteousness, for my salvation is near (Isa 56:1). Righteousness flows from God through the new birth: everyone who practices righteousness has been born of him (1Jo 2:29); whoever practices righteousness is righteous, as he is righteous (1Jo 3:7) — the person's righteousness corresponds to and flows from God's own. The kingdom of God is righteousness and peace and joy in the Holy Spirit (Rom 14:17). Whether it is right in the sight of God to listen to you rather than to God — the God-frame is the ultimate test of what is right (Act 4:19). |  |

| DIM-98-002 | DIMENSION_REVIEW |  | The dikaios/dikaiosunē/dikaiōsis/dikaioō family (G1342, G1343, G1344, G1347) shows a consistent tension: righteousness as divine act/attribute (Theological/Divine-Human) vs righteousness as human inner quality (Moral/Conscience). This is one of the most analytically significant tensions in the programme. Session B must examine this carefully. |

**Findings — via anchor verse (14 unique)**

| Finding ID | Type | Matched anchor refs | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| DIM-42-001 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | Phili 4:8 | The delight vocabulary spans every dimension of the inner being. This breadth suggests delight is not a single category but a family of engagements organised around the axis of what the inner person is oriented toward. Session B analysis should map this orientation axis as the structuring principle of the registry. |
| OBS-103-T2-008 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Mat 5:45 | The data provides an unusually direct answer. Joh 13:35 (OBS-103-129) — 'by this all people will know that you are my disciples, if you have love for one another' — makes love the externally legible mark of the divine image in the community. 1Jo 4:8 (via flag) — 'whoever does not love does not know God, for God is love' — states that lovelessness constitutes non-knowledge of God. The absence of love is not merely a moral deficiency; it is an epistemic and ontological condition: the person who does not love does not know God and therefore does not bear the divine image in its proper operation. Conversely, the presence of love — particularly love of enemies (Mat 5:44-45, OBS-103-144) — constitutes identity as a son/daughter of God. Love is thus the diagnostic marker of the divine image's condition: present and active = image bearing; absent or disordered = image distorted. |
| OBS-103-T2-041 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Yes — the great commandment includes 'with all your mind' (dianoia — Mat 22:37, OBS-103-139), explicitly locating love at the mind level. Phili 4:8 (OBS-103-133) directs the mind to attend to what is lovely (prosfilēs) — love's aesthetic quality becomes the object of cognitive direction. Joh 15:15 (OBS-103-052) — love constituted by knowledge-disclosure — places the cognitive (knowledge) at the centre of the love-relationship. Hos 6:6 (OBS-103-091) — God desires 'knowledge of God' alongside chesed — the knowledge-love co-priority is a mind-engagement. The mind's engagement is primarily as the organ of knowledge that love opens access to (friendship = mutual knowledge) and as the faculty directed to attend to the lovely. |
| OBS-103-T2-042 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Mind-engagement reveals that love has a genuine cognitive dimension — it is not sub-cognitive. The cognitive dimension operates in three ways: (a) **knowledge as love's medium** — friendship/love constituted by mutual knowledge-sharing (Joh 15:15) means love opens access to the inner world of the other and requires the mind's engagement to receive what is shared; (b) **cognitive direction toward the lovely** — Phili 4:8 makes the mind's attentiveness to what is lovely a cognitive act in service of love's orientation; (c) **knowledge of God as love's fruit** — Hos 6:6 and Jer 31:34 (OBS-103-072) place knowledge of God as the companion of love, with the new covenant completing the knowledge that love currently mediates. Love is cognitively demanding in the biblical data — it requires and produces knowledge. |
| OBS-103-T2-049 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Love is the programme's most fully integrated constitutional characteristic. The integration: at the spirit/will level — love is commanded (Mat 22:37), covenantal (chesed), and willed (enemy-love, Mat 5:44); at the soul level — love is experienced (Song 5:6 — soul failing at beloved's absence), given (Joh 15:13 — soul laid down for friends), and constitutive of identity (nephesh of God has a beloved, Jer 12:7); at the heart level — love is inscribed (Pro 3:3), infused by the Spirit (Rom 5:5), and the seat of love-awareness (Song 5:2); at the mind level — love requires and produces knowledge (Joh 15:15, Hos 6:6), and directs cognitive attention to the lovely (Phili 4:8); at the body level — love is expressed through kiss, embrace, tears, the movement of inner organs (Unit 7 multiple instances). No constitutional level is absent from the love vocabulary. Love is the programme's most constitutionally comprehensive characteristic — it operates at all levels simultaneously. |
| OBS-103-T2-058 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Yes. Phili 4:8 (OBS-103-133) is the clearest evidence: 'whatever is lovely (prosfilēs), think about these things' — love's aesthetic quality becomes the object that the attention-faculty (logizesthe — reckon, take account of) is commanded to attend to. Love shapes what the person notices and regards as worth attending to. Song 5:2 (OBS-103-108): the heart's wakefulness while the person sleeps — the beloved becomes the first object of perception even in the liminal state between sleep and waking. Joh 15:19 (OBS-103-150): the world loves its own — in-group love shapes the perception of who belongs and who is other. Love as a perceptual lens: what is loved becomes salient; what is not loved becomes invisible or threatening. |
| OBS-103-T2-142 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Love primarily **enables and deepens** the perceptive faculty. It enables: love for God opens the person to perceive what others cannot — Hos 6:6 (OBS-103-091) pairs knowledge of God with chesed, implying love as the condition for spiritual perception of God's ways. Love deepens: Phili 4:8 (OBS-103-133) — the mind directed to attend to what is lovely (prosfilēs) — love sharpens the inner sense for what is worth perceiving. Joh 15:15 (OBS-103-052): Jesus' love-disclosure opens the disciples' perception to what he has heard from the Father. Conversely, disordered love **impairs** perception: Luk 16:14 (OBS-103-136) — the Pharisees' love-of-money produces rejection of what they hear; Joh 3:19 (OBS-103-147) — love of darkness blinds to the light. The direction of love determines whether the perceptive faculty is opened or closed. |
| OBS-103-T2-143 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | The pattern reveals that love functions as a perceptual lens — it determines what the inner person sees as real and significant. Rightly directed love opens perception to what is true and lovely; misdirected love closes perception to correction and light. This means love is not merely affective but epistemic: it shapes the person's capacity to perceive. This is consistent with the epistemic finding in Q&A-153 (L-012): love constitutes the relational structure within which knowing becomes possible. The pattern also reveals that love's perceptual function is asymmetric — righteous love opens perception outward (toward God, the beloved, the truth); disordered love closes perception inward (toward the self and its preferred objects). Love and perception are constitutively linked: you see what you love. |
| OBS-064-T2-168 | OBSERVATION | 1Jo 1:9 | Inner conditions for receiving forgiveness: (1) Faith — Act 10:43 ("everyone who believes in him receives forgiveness"); (2) Repentance — Act 2:38 ("repent and be baptized... for the forgiveness of your sins"); (3) Confession — 1Jo 1:9 ("if we confess our sins, he is faithful and just to forgive"). These are access-postures, not merit-conditions — they name the inner orientation through which forgiveness is received, not the ground on which it is earned. OBS-064-022. |
| OBS-064-T2-217 | OBSERVATION | 1Jo 1:9 | Yes — conscience is profoundly engaged. The pre-forgiveness guilt state (OBS-064-013 — referenced from the forgiveness corpus context) is the conscience's verdict on the offender's condition. 1Jo 1:9 (confession → forgiveness → cleansing) shows conscience delivering the verdict (confession = conscience's public acknowledgement) and forgiveness addressing it. OBS-064-013, OBS-064-022. |
| OBS-064-T2-229 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 1Jo 1:9 | Yes — the seeking-forgiveness direction (human seeking divine forgiveness) is richly evidenced: Dan 9:19 (Daniel's intercession), Psa 130:4 (waiting for the Lord with hope for forgiveness), 1Jo 1:9 (confession → forgiveness), Act 2:38 (repentance for forgiveness). OBS-064-015, OBS-064-020, OBS-064-022. |
| OBS-064-T2-230 | OBSERVATION | 1Jo 1:9 | The inner posture required for the human-to-God direction: dependence (Dan 9:9 — grounding the plea in God's character, not human merit), honest self-assessment (1Jo 1:9 — confession names the condition accurately), and hope in divine forgiveness-character (Psa 130:4 — waiting for the Lord because forgiveness is with him). The posture is constitutively non-merit-claiming. OBS-064-016, OBS-064-022. |
| OBS-064-T2-231 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 1Jo 1:9 | The human-to-God direction reveals the person as constitutively dependent on divine forgiveness for restored relational access. The act of seeking forgiveness names the person's honest position: one who owes a debt they cannot pay and appeals to the one who can cancel it. This is a specific form of the Dependence/Creatureliness dimension — not general creaturely dependence but specifically moral-debt dependence. OBS-064-022. |
| OBS-064-T2-280 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | 1Jo 1:9 | Yes — multiple supplication/seeking terms. Dan 9:19 ("O Lord, forgive" — direct supplication using H5545 imperative). Act 2:38 (Peter's command to "be baptized for the forgiveness of your sins" — which responds to the crowd's "what shall we do?" as supplication). 1Jo 1:9 (confession as the seeking-act). The seeking of forgiveness is a rich vocabulary event — petition, confession, intercession. OBS-064-015, OBS-064-022. |

Session B analysis should map this orientation axis as the structuring principle of the registry. |
| OBS-103-T2-018 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 1Cor 13:5 | 1Cor 13:4-7 provides the programme's most explicit catalogue of what love excludes: envy, boasting, arrogance, rudeness, self-seeking, irritability, resentfulness, rejoicing in wrongdoing (OBS-103-127). The list names love's resistances across moral, relational, and emotional registers. At the structural level, love resists division of ultimate allegiance (Jam 4:4 — cannot love God and world simultaneously, OBS-103-058), resists self-assertion as the primary orientation (love-of-self as the first item in the last-days catalogue, OBS-103-137), and resists the withholding of self (Joh 15:13 — love's maximum is the giving of the soul/life, OBS-103-125). Love also explicitly resists concealment of inner truth from the beloved: Lev 19:17 contrasts hate-as-silence with love-as-frank-correction (OBS-103-114). |
| OBS-103-T2-028 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Jam 2:23 | From the data: (a) **obedience** — 1Pe 1:22 (OBS-103-124): soul-purification through obedience enables sincere love. The purified soul is the condition for authentic love. (b) **faith** — Jam 2:23 (OBS-103-053): Abraham's faith is what constitutes him as 'friend of God.' Faith as the inner condition through which love-relationship with God becomes possible. (c) **openness / not loving the darkness** — Joh 3:19 (OBS-103-147): those who love darkness cannot receive the light. The inner orientation away from evil is the negative condition for receiving love. (d) **being the returning prodigal** — Luk 15:20 (OBS-103-057): the son's return (movement toward the father) is the condition for the father's embrace. Some movement toward the source of love appears to be a condition for its reception. Gap: the data does not contain an explicit systematic account of reception-conditions. The conditions above are inferred from contexts where love is given and received. The gap is noted. |
| OBS-103-T2-029 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 1Cor 13:5 | The data names several inner blockers: (a) **love of darkness** — Joh 3:19 (OBS-103-147): the inner orientation toward darkness is the condition that prevents reception of love-as-light. The person who loves the darkness does not receive the light; the blocker is the prior love-commitment to a wrong object. (b) **love of money** — 1Ti 6:10 (OBS-103-132): love of money causes departure from faith — the love of a wrong object displaces the reception of divine love. (c) **pride/self-seeking** — 1Cor 13:4-7 (OBS-103-127): love of self expressed as arrogance and self-seeking is explicitly named as what love is not — the self-orientation blocks the other-orientation that genuine love requires. (d) **false-brotherhood orientation** — Gal 2:4 (OBS-103-068): the concealed inner agenda of the false brother prevents genuine love-reception within the community. The pattern: all inner blockers are disordered loves — prior commitments to wrong objects that displace the capacity for rightly directed love. |
| OBS-103-T2-041 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Yes — the great commandment includes 'with all your mind' (dianoia — Mat 22:37, OBS-103-139), explicitly locating love at the mind level. Phili 4:8 (OBS-103-133) directs the mind to attend to what is lovely (prosfilēs) — love's aesthetic quality becomes the object of cognitive direction. Joh 15:15 (OBS-103-052) — love constituted by knowledge-disclosure — places the cognitive (knowledge) at the centre of the love-relationship. Hos 6:6 (OBS-103-091) — God desires 'knowledge of God' alongside chesed — the knowledge-love co-priority is a mind-engagement. The mind's engagement is primarily as the organ of knowledge that love opens access to (friendship = mutual knowledge) and as the faculty directed to attend to the lovely. |
| OBS-103-T2-042 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Mind-engagement reveals that love has a genuine cognitive dimension — it is not sub-cognitive. The cognitive dimension operates in three ways: (a) **knowledge as love's medium** — friendship/love constituted by mutual knowledge-sharing (Joh 15:15) means love opens access to the inner world of the other and requires the mind's engagement to receive what is shared; (b) **cognitive direction toward the lovely** — Phili 4:8 makes the mind's attentiveness to what is lovely a cognitive act in service of love's orientation; (c) **knowledge of God as love's fruit** — Hos 6:6 and Jer 31:34 (OBS-103-072) place knowledge of God as the companion of love, with the new covenant completing the knowledge that love currently mediates. Love is cognitively demanding in the biblical data — it requires and produces knowledge. |
| OBS-103-T2-049 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Love is the programme's most fully integrated constitutional characteristic. The integration: at the spirit/will level — love is commanded (Mat 22:37), covenantal (chesed), and willed (enemy-love, Mat 5:44); at the soul level — love is experienced (Song 5:6 — soul failing at beloved's absence), given (Joh 15:13 — soul laid down for friends), and constitutive of identity (nephesh of God has a beloved, Jer 12:7); at the heart level — love is inscribed (Pro 3:3), infused by the Spirit (Rom 5:5), and the seat of love-awareness (Song 5:2); at the mind level — love requires and produces knowledge (Joh 15:15, Hos 6:6), and directs cognitive attention to the lovely (Phili 4:8); at the body level — love is expressed through kiss, embrace, tears, the movement of inner organs (Unit 7 multiple instances). No constitutional level is absent from the love vocabulary. Love is the programme's most constitutionally comprehensive characteristic — it operates at all levels simultaneously. |
| OBS-103-T2-058 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Yes. Phili 4:8 (OBS-103-133) is the clearest evidence: 'whatever is lovely (prosfilēs), think about these things' — love's aesthetic quality becomes the object that the attention-faculty (logizesthe — reckon, take account of) is commanded to attend to. Love shapes what the person notices and regards as worth attending to. Song 5:2 (OBS-103-108): the heart's wakefulness while the person sleeps — the beloved becomes the first object of perception even in the liminal state between sleep and waking. Joh 15:19 (OBS-103-150): the world loves its own — in-group love shapes the perception of who belongs and who is other. Love as a perceptual lens: what is loved becomes salient; what is not loved becomes invisible or threatening. |
| OBS-103-T2-084 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 1Cor 13:5 | Yes. Joh 15:15 (OBS-103-052): the disciples are transformed from servants to friends through Christ's love — a fundamental identity and relational reclassification. 1Pe 1:22 (OBS-103-124): soul-purification as the outcome of the love-formation process — the inner person is changed by the pathway that leads to sincere love. The 1Cor 13 trajectory (OBS-103-127): love's sustained exercise produces a character that does not envy, boast, or seek its own — the moral character is transformed by the practice of love. Luk 7:47 (via SD-020): the woman who loves much has been transformed by forgiveness — the transformation in her produced the love-expression. Love both requires and produces transformation. |
| OBS-103-T2-142 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | Love primarily **enables and deepens** the perceptive faculty. It enables: love for God opens the person to perceive what others cannot — Hos 6:6 (OBS-103-091) pairs knowledge of God with chesed, implying love as the condition for spiritual perception of God's ways. Love deepens: Phili 4:8 (OBS-103-133) — the mind directed to attend to what is lovely (prosfilēs) — love sharpens the inner sense for what is worth perceiving. Joh 15:15 (OBS-103-052): Jesus' love-disclosure opens the disciples' perception to what he has heard from the Father. Conversely, disordered love **impairs** perception: Luk 16:14 (OBS-103-136) — the Pharisees' love-of-money produces rejection of what they hear; Joh 3:19 (OBS-103-147) — love of darkness blinds to the light. The direction of love determines whether the perceptive faculty is opened or closed. |
| OBS-103-T2-143 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Phili 4:8 | The pattern reveals that love functions as a perceptual lens — it determines what the inner person sees as real and significant. Rightly directed love opens perception to what is true and lovely; misdirected love closes perception to correction and light. This means love is not merely affective but epistemic: it shapes the person's capacity to perceive. This is consistent with the epistemic finding in Q&A-153 (L-012): love constitutes the relational structure within which knowing becomes possible. The pattern also reveals that love's perceptual function is asymmetric — righteous love opens perception outward (toward God, the beloved, the truth); disordered love closes perception inward (toward the self and its preferred objects). Love and perception are constitutively linked: you see what you love. |
| OBS-103-T2-167 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 1Cor 13:5 | Love plays a dual role in the sanctification arc: it is both the **process** and the **goal**. As process: love operating through the life — obedience, community, training — is itself sanctifying. Pro 3:3's heart-inscription implies that sustained exercise of chesed changes the person over time into the kind of person whose heart has it written into it. Joh 14:15 (OBS-103-140): sustained love of Christ produces the commandment-keeping pattern that constitutes sanctification's behavioural dimension. As goal: the 1Cor 13 portrait is the telos of sanctification — a person who loves with no envy, arrogance, or self-seeking is the sanctification's outcome. 1Jo 4:18 (via SP-103-029): 'perfect love casts out fear' — perfect love is the eschatological form of sanctification's completion. Love is not one virtue among the list of sanctification's outputs; it is the organising content from which all other virtue flows and toward which sanctification moves. |

### G1344 dikaioō — to justify (`mti_id=3194`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `G1344` · Lang: Greek · Owner: OWNER
2. Registry: R098 justice · Legacy C-cluster: C13
3. Sub-group: `M26-C` (Justification: Declaring or Making Righteous)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 71
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=3194` · `md_version=1` · `vc_status=not_done`
6. Mounce short-def: to justify, vindicate, declare righteous, to put someone in a proper relationship with another, usually referring to God’s relationship to humankind, implying a proper legal or moral relationship

**Verses:** 36 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (1)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1427 | 3194-001 | Term names the divine act of justification — the declaration that transforms the person's inner moral standing before God, received through faith |  |

**Findings — direct (Strong's mention) (1)**

| Finding ID | Type | Anchor verses | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| DIM-98-002 | DIMENSION_REVIEW |  | The dikaios/dikaiosunē/dikaiōsis/dikaioō family (G1342, G1343, G1344, G1347) shows a consistent tension: righteousness as divine act/attribute (Theological/Divine-Human) vs righteousness as human inner quality (Moral/Conscience). This is one of the most analytically significant tensions in the programme. Session B must examine this carefully. |

**Findings — via anchor verse (5 unique)**

| Finding ID | Type | Matched anchor refs | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| DIM-19-001 | DIMENSION_REVIEW | Rom 8:30 | The calling registry (Reg 19) has 13 of 24 groups (54%) assigned Divine-Human Correspondence — the highest concentration of any single registry in the programme to date. The calling vocabulary is structurally a divine-human act: God calls, names, and constitutes; the human receives, responds, and is formed. Session B should examine how the human inner person is changed by the divine call, and what distinguishes the groups where HUMAN volition or emotion is the primary inner-being phenomenon from those where the divine calling act is the governing feature. |
| OBS-117-T2-010 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 5:1 | Yes — and structurally so. The OT vocabulary names peace as a condition to be given, a covenant to be kept, a king to come. The NT vocabulary names peace as a person present: G1515 group 426-003 (Eph 2:14 — "he himself is our peace," OBS-117-122 from prior session). The Greek eirēnē carries all the semantic range of shalom (established by the LXX translation pattern) and then names Christ as the one in whom all those dimensions converge. The NT fullness: (1) Peace with God (Rom 5:1) — the relational condition; (2) Peace of God guarding hearts and minds (Phili 4:7) — the inner condition; (3) Christ IS peace (Eph 2:14) — the person; (4) Spirit-fruit peace (Gal 5:22) — the gift; (5) cosmic peace-making through the blood of the cross (Col 1:20, G1517 group 430-001) — the mechanism. The OT's four shalom dimensions (felt state, relational condition, divine gift, eschatological reality) are not rejected in the NT but fulfilled in a single person. |
| OBS-117-T2-013 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 5:1 | Strong continuity. The LXX translates shalom with eirēnē throughout the Hebrew Bible, establishing the semantic correspondence as the foundational datum of the NT peace vocabulary. The NT eirēnē carries the full shalom range: (1) Inner felt state (Phili 4:7 — "the peace of God... will guard your hearts and your minds," corresponding to shalom 2508-001); (2) Covenantal gift (Rom 5:1 — "peace with God through our Lord Jesus Christ," corresponding to 2508-002); (3) Moral orientation (Eirēneuō, G1514 — "live peaceably with all," corresponding to 2508-003); (4) Messianic embodiment (Eph 2:14 — "he himself is our peace," corresponding to 2508-004). The correspondence is not merely semantic but structural — the NT's peace vocabulary is the OT's shalom vocabulary transposed into the key of Christ. No significant discontinuity; the NT deepens rather than revises. |
| OBS-117-T2-015 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 5:1 | All four, in structured combination: (1) State: shalom as the inner condition of the person (2508-001 — inner peace as felt experience; 2509-001 — welfare as existential condition); (2) Act: shalom as active orientation and pursuit (2508-003 — "I am for peace"; Psa 34:14 — "seek peace and pursue it"; G1514 eirēneuō — actively being at peace with others); (3) Quality: shalom as the stable character quality of the peaceable person (Mal 2:6 — walked in peace and uprightness; G1516 eirēnikos — peaceableness as quality of heavenly wisdom; G2272 — gentle and quiet spirit as character); (4) Relationship: shalom as the condition of the God-human relationship (2508-002 — covenant peace; G1517 — making peace through the blood of Christ; Rom 5:1 — peace with God). The evidence does not privilege any one of these over the others — they are structurally interdependent. The relational dimension (covenant) is the ground; the state dimension (felt condition) is the fruit; the quality dimension (character) is the formed expression; the act dimension (pursuit, peacemaking) is the volitional engagement. |
| OBS-117-T2-117 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Rom 5:1 | Present peace is explicitly described as anticipatory: (1) Phili 4:7 — "the peace of God which surpasses all understanding will guard your hearts and minds" — the present peace is already beyond cognitive comprehension but not yet the fullness; (2) Rom 5:1 — "peace with God" as the present justified state is the foretaste of the eschatological reconciliation; (3) 2Th 1:7 — "relief to you who are afflicted when the Lord Jesus is revealed" — present peace under affliction anticipates the eschatological relief-from-all-affliction; (4) Rev 8:1 — the half-hour silence in heaven is the eschatological fullness of the demamah-silence pattern (1Ki 19:12) — the present silence before God's voice anticipates the cosmic silence before the final unveiling. Present peace is a genuine foretaste of future fullness, not merely a symbol — it is the same peace (peace of God, Phili 4:7) experienced partially rather than a different or lesser peace. |
