**Findings — direct (Strong's mention) (0)**

(none)

**Findings — via anchor verse (29 unique)**

| Finding ID | Type | Matched anchor refs | Excerpt |
|---|---|---|---|
| OBS-067-T2-005 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 2Th 1:11 | The evidence is suggestive but not fully explicit. 2Th 1:11 ("may God fulfil every resolve for good and every work of faith by his power") implies an eschatological completeness — the resolve for good is present now, but its fulfilment is anticipated as God's completing act. Eph 2:7 places God's display of chrēstotēs "in the coming ages" — goodness has a future-facing dimension of fullness. Gal 5:22 as Spirit-fruit connects goodness to the present work of the Spirit that anticipates the fullness of Spirit-transformation. However, the data does not contain a verse that explicitly narrates goodness as moving toward eschatological fullness in the way that glory or resurrection does. The eschatological trajectory is implied by the pneumatological framework (Spirit-production → Spirit-completion) but not explicitly named. This is a partial gap. (OBS-067-OBS-008, OBS-067-OBS-016) |
| OBS-067-T2-025 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | The sustained effects named in the verse evidence: (1) Stable inner orientation rooted in nearness to God — Psa 73:28: Asaph's crisis of doubt resolved into a permanently reoriented inner life ("it is good to be near God"). This is not a momentary feeling but a repositioned inner orientation. (2) Community-building capacity — Rom 15:14: persons "full of goodness" are "able to instruct one another" — sustained goodness produces the capacity to contribute to the formation of others. (3) Settled dispositional kindness toward others — Col 3:12: chrēstotēs as "garment" of the renewed person — a sustained character quality, not an occasional kindness. (4) Completed righteous resolve — 2Th 1:11: God's sustained work fulfils "every resolve for good" — over time, the person's Spirit-enabled goodness is completed toward its intended expression. (OBS-067-OBS-029, OBS-067-OBS-040, SBF-067-Q039) |
| OBS-067-T2-027 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | Yes — there is a clear distinction. The immediate response to encountering divine goodness is trust/refuge (Psa 34:8) and praise (doxological group). The sustained effect is repositioned inner orientation (Psa 73:28) and community capacity (Rom 15:14). The immediate response is reactive — a first movement of the inner being toward the good encountered. The sustained effect is constitutive — a settled state that reshapes the person's character, orientation, and relational capacity over time. The distinction is most sharply drawn in Psa 73 where Asaph's encounter with divine goodness produces a sustained reorientation rather than a momentary response. 2Th 1:11's "fulfil every resolve for good" confirms the sustained/completion dimension — goodness works toward completion, not just initiation. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-029, SBF-067-Q031) |
| OBS-067-T2-052 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | 2Th 1:11 | The sequence is: (1) Divine source acts (God's goodness, Spirit's production, God's word) → (2) Spirit-level reception in the inner being (Spirit-fruit, infilling with goodness) → (3) Soul reorientation (Psa 73:28 — repositioned toward God; Lam 3:25 — the soul seeking) → (4) Heart engagement (knowing, gladness, moral recognition) → (5) Volitional alignment (choosing good, willing submission, Jer 26:14) → (6) Outward expression (conduct, justice, instruction of others). This is a formation sequence, not an instantaneous transformation. The 2Th 1:11 language ("fulfil every resolve for good") implies that step 5 (volitional resolve) is followed by step 6 (completion by God's power) — the full sequence requires both human resolve and divine completion. (OBS-067-OBS-016, OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040, SBF-067-Q031) |
| OBS-067-T2-065 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | 2Th 1:11 | Yes — Volition (04) is a confirmed secondary dimension. The volitional engagement is extensive. 884-008 (40 verses, volitional preference idiom, anchor Jer 26:14) is the single largest group: the "good-in-one's-eyes" / "what seems good to you" formula names goodness as the predicate of volitional choice and evaluative agreement. 885-001 (Spirit-produced goodness as resolve): agathōsunē in 2Th 1:11 is "every resolve for good" — the Spirit enables the will toward goodness. Goodness engages volition both as the criterion (what the will should choose) and as the reconstituted capacity (Spirit-enabled resolve toward goodness). Two groups carry Volition: 884-008 (Hebrew volitional-preference) and 885-001 (Greek Spirit-enabled resolve). (OBS-067-OBS-014, OBS-067-OBS-016, OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040) |
| OBS-067-T2-066 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | Goodness reconstitutes volition rather than bypassing or overriding it. 2Th 1:11 makes this precise: "may God fulfil every resolve for good" — the human resolve is real and operative; God's power fulfils it (does not replace it). The structure is: Spirit-produced goodness produces a genuine resolve in the human will → God's power fulfils that resolve → the human agent acts. The will's capacity is enabled (Spirit-production enables the resolve), the will's interaction with other characteristics is shaped (goodness reconstitutes what the will chooses as its object), and the will operates under the constraint of the good as revealed (Mic 6:8 — "He has told you what is good" sets the constraint within which the will's proper operation is defined). Volition without this reconstitution produces self-direction (Isa 65:2) or wilful refusal (Jer 6:16) — the will's natural operation in the fallen condition is away from the good. (OBS-067-OBS-014, OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040, SBF-067-Q040) |
| OBS-067-T2-067 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | The volitional engagement reveals that goodness is not merely a character quality but an active principle that must be chosen and willed. Goodness does not produce compliance or passive absorption — it produces resolve ("every resolve for good," 2Th 1:11) and active choosing ("do what seems good," 884-008). The person who possesses goodness chooses it actively — Col 3:12 frames this as "put on therefore" — a deliberate volitional act enabled by prior transformation. This reveals that goodness has a volitional depth that distinguishes it from passive character states: it is both a quality possessed and a quality actively willed. The reconstitution of volition by goodness means that the good person is not merely compliant but genuinely desirous of the good — the will itself has been reoriented rather than overridden. (OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040, SBF-067-Q040) |
| OBS-067-T2-068 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | Agency is engaged and required. Mic 6:8 requires active human doing — "to do justice, and to love kindness, and to walk humbly." The goodness defined here is not a state but a practice — it requires the person to initiate and sustain action. The "garment" language of Col 3:12 ("put on therefore... kindness") frames goodness as an active choice to take up — an agentive act. Pro 16:32 ("he who rules his spirit than he who takes a city") names self-governance as an agency act with positive moral value. 2Th 1:11's "every resolve for good" is a person's own resolve — the agentive capacity is intact and engaged. Goodness does not bypass agency; it requires it and enables it. (OBS-067-OBS-027, OBS-067-OBS-040, SBF-067-Q040) |
| OBS-067-T2-069 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | Goodness enables and deepens agency. The reconstitution pattern (Q&A-082): Spirit-produced goodness produces a genuine resolve that the human agent executes, with God's power completing it (2Th 1:11). This is not a reduction of agency but an amplification of it — the Spirit-enabled person has a deeper capacity for agentive goodness than the unregenerate person (whose agency is misdirected toward self-direction, Isa 65:2). Col 3:12's "put on" is an agency act — the Spirit's prior transformation is the enabling ground, and the human agentive choice is the expression. Goodness also provides the criterion for agency's proper exercise (Mic 6:8 — doing justice and loving kindness require active choosing and doing). (OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040, OBS-067-OBS-027) |
| OBS-067-T2-070 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | The agency engagement reveals that goodness is an active, outward-directed characteristic — it does not rest in inner states but reaches toward expression. The six-step constitutional movement identified at Q&A-064 culminates in outward action (step 6: expression in conduct, justice, instruction). The deepening of agency through goodness confirms that genuine goodness is not quietist or passive — it is an inner quality that demands expression. The contrast with the fallen condition (Rom 3:12: "no one does good, not even one" — agency directed away from the good) confirms that the agency-restoration produced by goodness is a constitutive element of the characteristic's full expression. (OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040, OBS-067-OBS-046) |
| OBS-067-T2-077 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | Conscientiousness is the integrating faculty that brings together moral awareness, will, and action — and goodness is one of the most comprehensively integrated characteristics in the programme precisely at this level. Mic 6:8 is the clearest evidence: what is good is defined as doing justice (action), loving kindness (dispositional-volitional), and walking humbly with God (orientational) — the tri-fold definition requires the simultaneous engagement of moral awareness (knowing what is good), volition (choosing and loving it), and sustained action (walking, doing). Col 3:12's "put on therefore... kindness" similarly integrates moral awareness (recognition of one's transformed identity), volition (the act of putting on), and action (expressing kindness). Conscientiousness is the faculty that holds these three together in integrated lived expression — and goodness requires and produces this integration. (OBS-067-OBS-027, OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040) |
| OBS-067-T2-107 | VERSE_PATTERN | 2Th 1:11 | Yes — the formation arc is evidenced in the NT evidence. Col 3:12–14 places the "putting on" of chrēstotēs within a longer paraenetic arc: the person has already received new identity transformation ("God's chosen ones, holy and beloved"); is now to clothe themselves with the character qualities that express this identity (3:12); culminating in love that binds everything together in perfect harmony (3:14). This is a formation sequence — the Spirit's prior work, the intentional character-expression, and the sustained orientation toward love as the integrating whole. Gal 5:22–23 similarly places goodness within the fruit of the Spirit — a cluster of nine character qualities that are the full expression of Spirit-formed character. The "fruit" metaphor implies organic growth over time — formation, not instantaneous achievement. (OBS-067-OBS-017, OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-039) |
| OBS-067-T2-109 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 2Th 1:11 | Partial eschatological evidence is present. Eph 2:7 ("so that in the coming ages he might show the immeasurable riches of his grace in kindness toward us in Christ Jesus") explicitly places chrēstotēs in a future-oriented eschatological frame — God's chrēstotēs will be displayed in its fullness in the coming ages. This names an eschatological trajectory: present experience of divine kindness anticipates its future unrestricted display. 2Th 1:11 ("fulfil every resolve for good") implies a completion — God's completing act brings present goodness-resolve to its fullness. However, the specifically eschatological texture of goodness (what it will be in the new creation, what fullness means) is not directly described in the anchor verses beyond these two instances. The trajectory is indicated but not fully developed. (OBS-067-OBS-008, OBS-067-OBS-016, SBF-067-Q006) |
| OBS-067-T2-110 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | 2Th 1:11 | Eph 2:7 implies that present experience of chrēstotēs is a foretaste of the "immeasurable riches" of its future expression — present goodness is real but partial relative to its coming fullness. The "immeasurable" qualifier suggests that the eschatological form of goodness exceeds present experience in scale and richness. 2Th 1:11's "fulfil every resolve for good" implies that present resolves for goodness, even when genuine, are not yet complete — their completion is a divine eschatological act. The present experience of goodness therefore anticipates: (1) A fullness of divine kindness-display that surpasses present experience; (2) A completion of human goodness-resolve that requires divine power to finish. The gap is that the data does not describe what the experiential content of eschatological goodness will be — only that it will be fuller and completed. (OBS-067-OBS-008, OBS-067-OBS-016) |
| OBS-067-T2-113 | CROSS_REGISTRY | 2Th 1:11 | Two sequential relationships are directly named: (1) Goodness → repentance (Rom 2:4): "God's kindness is meant to lead you to repentance" — chrēstotēs explicitly precedes and produces repentance. The sequence is causal: divine goodness is prior, repentance follows. (2) Goodness → faith/trust (Psa 34:8): encounter with divine goodness ("taste and see that the Lord is good") is followed by "blessed is the man who takes refuge in him" — goodness is the experiential prior that produces the faith/refuge response. Goodness precedes faith in the encounter sequence. A third sequence is implied by SBF-067-Q133 (prior analysis): goodness is prior to faith (encounter with goodness → faith response); 2Th 1:11 shows them as parallel co-products in the other direction. These sequential findings are confirmed and structured. (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-043, SBF-067-Q127, SBF-067-Q133) |
| OBS-067-T2-114 | CROSS_REGISTRY | 2Th 1:11 | The goodness → repentance sequence (Rom 2:4) is explicitly causal: "is meant to lead to" — divine chrēstotēs is the mechanism producing repentance. The goodness → faith sequence (Psa 34:8) is causal-enabling: the experiential encounter with goodness enables and produces the trust response. The goodness-and-faith parallel sequence (2Th 1:11: "every resolve for good and every work of faith") is correlational — they are co-produced by God's power rather than one causing the other. The three sequential relationships reveal three different structural relationships: causal (goodness produces repentance), enabling-causal (goodness enables faith-response), and correlational (goodness-resolve and faith-work as parallel Spirit-expressions). (OBS-067-OBS-025, OBS-067-OBS-043, SBF-067-Q127) |
| OBS-067-T2-116 | CROSS_REGISTRY | 2Th 1:11 | Human goodness is produced by: (1) The Spirit — Spirit-production is the primary mechanism for NT goodness (Gal 5:22; 885-001, 886-002). The Spirit is the enabling cause of agathōsunē and chrēstotēs as inner qualities of the believer. (2) Encounter with divine goodness — the person who tastes divine goodness and is reoriented toward God develops the capacity for goodness (Psa 73:28; encounter → orientation → goodness). (3) God's power completing human resolve — 2Th 1:11: "God fulfil every resolve for good" — the human resolve is real but God's power is the completing cause of the resolve's expression. These are not competing causes but a layered causal structure: God's goodness → Spirit's production → human reception and reorientation → expressed goodness. (OBS-067-OBS-016, OBS-067-OBS-039, OBS-067-OBS-040) |
| OBS-067-T2-137 | ETYMOLOGY | 2Th 1:11 | G0019 (agathōsunē) is almost certainly a NT coinage — not attested in classical Greek or LXX (SBF-067-Q099). Its entire 4-verse NT corpus (Rom 15:14, Gal 5:22, Eph 5:9, 2Th 1:11) is Pauline and pneumatological. The coinage reveals: the NT needed a term for goodness as a named inner-being virtue distinct from the adjective agathos that applies broadly. Agathōsunē says: goodness is now a specific inner-being quality, not merely a predicate applied to things. The development from H2896A's ten-sub-sense breadth to agathōsunē's four-verse precision is pneumatological concentration: goodness in its fullest expression named as a Spirit-enabled virtue. The coinage represents the NT's sharpening of the concept from broad phenomenological range (Hebrew) to named Spirit-fruit virtue (NT). (OBS-067-OBS-009, SBF-067-Q099) |
| OBS-034-T2-047 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | Heb 10:29 | Both directions are present, operating differently for each body-part link. (1) Blood — bidirectional: the blood is shed from the body outward (soul expresses through body in the act of sacrifice — the inner commitment of the covenant-maker is enacted through the body's blood); and the blood acts back upon the inner person constitutively (the covenant sealed in blood creates a bond that changes the inner standing and identity of those under it — Heb 10:29 mentions "the blood of the covenant by which he was sanctified" — OBS-034-070). The inner → body direction (sacrifice/outpouring) and the body → inner direction (sanctification/constituting) are both present. (2) Circumcision — inner → body: the inner covenant commitment (received from God, entered by the human) is expressed and inscribed outwardly on the body. The primary direction is soul → body: the covenantal identity is inscribed on the body as its sign. There is no clear body → soul direction for circumcision in the data (Paul explicitly decouples the external sign from inner significance in Rom 2:28–29, though that passage is not in the verse set). |
| OBS-034-T2-048 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | Heb 10:29 | The bidirectional blood-covenant relationship reveals that covenant is not merely inner and invisible — it moves through the body and back again, using physical substance as its medium. The consequence: covenant is constitutionally somatic in its enacted form; it cannot be reduced to a purely internal or invisible inner-being reality. The body participates in covenant both as the site of covenant's expression (sacrifice, circumcision, spoken oath) and as the medium through which covenant is constituted and marked. This has implications for understanding the new covenant's sacramental forms (Eucharist, baptism): both are bodily acts that carry covenant significance — they are not merely symbolic but participate in the covenant's constituting and marking logic that runs through the body. The body is not external to covenant but is the medium through which covenant is enacted, marked, and sustained in time. |
| OBS-034-T2-054 | SPIRIT_SOUL_BODY | Heb 10:29 | Yes — covenant shows constitutional movement across multiple levels. (1) Divine Spirit → human inner person: 2 Cor 3:6 (Spirit administers the new covenant — OBS-034-061); Isa 59:21 (God's Spirit placed upon the person, God's words placed in the mouth). The new covenant moves from the divine Spirit into the constitutional core of the human person. (2) Inner → somatic (soul → body): the inner covenantal commitment is expressed and enacted through bodily acts — oath (spoken), cutting ritual (bodily enactment — OBS-034-044), blood-shedding (sacrifice), circumcision (bodily inscription — OBS-034-053). The inner disposition moves outward into somatic expression. (3) Somatic → inner (body → soul): the blood of the covenant acts back upon the inner person constitutively — "by which he was sanctified" (Heb 10:29 — OBS-034-070); the cup of the new covenant received bodily (Lk 22:20) constitutes the covenantal bond. The body participates in covenant's constituting work, feeding back into the inner person. (4) Inner → word/mouth → generational transmission (OBS-034-051 direction, Isa 59:21): covenantal content moves from the inner person outward through the mouth and forward through generations. |
| OBS-034-T2-077 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Heb 10:29 | Yes — conscience is engaged structurally and specifically. (1) Dan 9:11 (OBS-034-036): Daniel's prayer is a conscience-driven confession — "we have sinned against him." The activated covenant curse is experienced consciously as guilt and judgment, and the response is confessional acknowledgment of conscience's testimony. (2) Heb 10:29 (OBS-034-070): "has trampled underfoot the Son of God, and has profaned the blood of the covenant by which he was sanctified, and has outraged the Spirit of grace" — the language describes covenant-profaning as a conscience-level violation; the seriousness of the act is measured by what it has done to the conscience's object (the sanctifying blood). (3) The curse structure (H0423 — OBS-034-007) engages conscience by naming covenant breach as an activation of self-incurred judgment — the person who breaks the covenant must live with the conscience-testimony that they have brought the curse upon themselves. (4) R073 (guilt, 20 shared co-occurrence verses — OBS-034-031): the frequent co-occurrence of guilt vocabulary with covenant vocabulary confirms that conscience-engagement is a regular feature of covenant contexts. |
| OBS-034-T2-078 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 10:29 | Covenant deepens and clarifies conscience — it does not bypass or impair it. (1) Deepening: covenant gives conscience a specific and weighty object — the person under covenant is not merely subject to a vague moral awareness but to conscience-testimony regarding specific covenantal obligations. The covenant's clarity (written law, specific obligations, sworn terms) makes conscience's work more precise. (2) The new covenant deepens conscience further by internalising the standard — law written on the heart (Heb 8:10) means conscience operates from within the person's own renewed moral constitution, not merely against an external standard. (3) The guilt co-occurrence data (OBS-034-031) confirms that covenant contexts regularly produce the conscience-awareness of failure — the covenant is the framework within which guilt is named and experienced. (4) The Hebrews argument (Heb 9:9; 10:1-2 context, though outside the verse set) implies that the old covenant's sacrificial system could not fully clear the conscience — the new covenant's superior blood does. This trajectory — old covenant engaging but not fully clearing conscience; new covenant engaging and clearing it — is implied by the data without being directly stated in the programme's verse set. |
| OBS-034-T2-079 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 10:29 | The pattern reveals that covenant creates the conditions under which conscience functions with maximum clarity and weight. Within covenant, the person knows precisely what they are accountable to (the covenant's terms), has undergone a solemn self-binding (the oath), and carries the knowledge that breach activates the embedded curse. This means conscience within covenant is heightened conscience — not a vague sense of wrongdoing but acute awareness of specific covenantal violation and its consequences. The 20 shared guilt co-occurrence verses (OBS-034-031) confirm this heightened conscience pattern quantitatively. The pattern also reveals that the new covenant's solution to the conscience problem (blood that sanctifies — Heb 10:29 — OBS-034-070) is not the elimination of conscience but the providing of a sufficient atoning answer to conscience's testimony. Covenant both activates and satisfies conscience. |
| OBS-034-T2-097 | MEANING_OBSERVATION | Heb 10:29 | Addressed in Q&A-033 (T1.7.3) — the inner-being state is: desolation (Eph 2:12 — OBS-034-060), bondage (Gal 4:24 — OBS-034-071), moral dissolution (Rom 1:31 — OBS-034-057), and curse-activation for those who entered but then rejected the covenant (Dan 9:11 — OBS-034-036; Heb 10:29 — OBS-034-070). Consistent with T1.7.3. No new findings — confirmed stable. |
| OBS-034-T2-104 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 10:29 | The evidence points in different directions for different covenantal modes. (1) The new covenant constitutional transformation (law on heart, Spirit-administration) — the evidence does not explicitly address reversibility for this mode. The language is declarative and permanent in intent: God says "I will" (future promise framing in Jer 31:31-34; Heb 8:8-10 — OBS-034-040). The covenant is described as "eternal" (Heb 13:20 — OBS-034-074), which implies irreversibility. (2) Covenant-breach as reversible rupture: the OT evidence shows covenant breach (Dan 9:11 — OBS-034-036) followed by potential restoration (God's own covenant-memory persists despite breach — Gen 9:15, Lk 1:72 — OBS-034-073). Covenant is broken but not permanently destroyed from God's side. (3) Heb 10:29 (OBS-034-070): "trampled underfoot the Son of God, profaned the blood of the covenant by which he was sanctified" — this language implies that profaning the covenant after receiving it represents a severe and serious condition, though the text does not explicitly define the limits of reversibility. Gap: the question of whether new covenant transformation is individually reversible is not directly answered in the programme's verse set. Noting as P. |
| OBS-034-T2-111 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 10:29 | Yes — covenant is the structural framework within which the entire arc of character formation and sanctification takes place in Scripture. (1) The Sinai covenant's law shapes moral character over time through sustained discipline and obedience — the person who keeps covenant ("keeps his commandments" — Deu 7:9; "keeps his covenant and his testimonies" — Psa 25:10 — OBS-034-039) is progressively formed into a person of integrity. (2) The new covenant constitutionally initiates this formation at a deeper level: law written on the heart (Heb 8:10 — OBS-034-059) is the constitutional foundation for ongoing moral formation. The Spirit's administration (2 Cor 3:6 — OBS-034-061) produces what the law demanded. Heb 10:29 mentions "sanctified by the blood of the covenant" (OBS-034-070) — the covenant blood is the ground of sanctification, not merely justification. (3) The new self/new creation (777-002 — OBS-034-062) — kainos in the register of formation: Eph 4:24 — "put on the new self, created after the likeness of God in true righteousness and holiness." Covenant produces formation toward Godlikeness. |
| OBS-034-T2-112 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | Heb 10:29 | Covenant plays a foundational, architecturally prior role in the arc of character formation and sanctification — it is not one element among equals but the structural framework that makes formation possible. Three specific roles: (1) Constitutional grounding — the new covenant's law-on-heart inscription (Heb 8:10) provides the inner constitutional ground from which all subsequent formation grows. Formation cannot proceed sustainably without this constitutional foundation. (2) Relational motivation — covenant positions the person in a relationship of known belonging ("I will be their God, they shall be my people" — Heb 8:10). Formation that flows from known belonging is qualitatively different from formation driven by fear of punishment. (3) Progressive sanctification through the blood — "sanctified by the blood of the covenant" (Heb 10:29 — OBS-034-070) — the covenant's atoning mechanism is the ongoing basis for the sanctification that formation requires. Without covenantal atonement, the moral weight of failure would prevent formation from proceeding. Covenant provides the structural conditions (constitution, belonging, atonement) within which formation operates. |
| OBS-034-T2-121 | CROSS_REGISTRY | Heb 10:29 | Yes — covenant produces several distinct characteristics as its effects. (1) Knowing God (R160 thought / cognition domain): Jer 31:34 — the new covenant inscription produces knowing God as its primary sustained effect (Q&A-073, OBS-034-040). (2) Hope (R078): Eph 2:12 — covenant inclusion constitutes the ground for hope; its absence produces hopelessness (OBS-034-060). Covenant produces hope as one of its inner-being effects. (3) Faithfulness / moral character (R060): sustained covenant-keeping produces the moral character quality of faithfulness — the oath-keeper develops the inner moral character of trustworthiness (3308-002 — OBS-034-053). Covenant produces faithfulness in the person who keeps it. (4) Freedom (related to Gal 4:24 — OBS-034-071): the new covenant produces inner freedom as opposed to the bondage produced by the old covenant's external-demand mechanism. (5) Sanctification (Heb 10:29 — OBS-034-070): "sanctified by the blood of the covenant" — the covenant blood produces the condition of sanctification in the person it covers. |

### G1556 ekdikeō — to avenge (`mti_id=944`)

**Identity & meaning**

1. Strong's: `G1556` · Lang: Greek · Owner: OWNER
2. Registry: R098 justice · Legacy C-cluster: C13
3. Sub-group: `M26-A2` (Human Righteousness — State of being right) · `M26-D` (Avenging Justice: Retribution for Wrongdoing)
4. Term status: extracted · Evidential: — · Occurrences: 71
5. Patch-authoring refs: `mti_id=944` · `md_version=1` · `vc_status=not_done`
6. Mounce short-def: to avenge, take revenge; to grant justice, get justice

**Verses:** 6 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (3)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1432 | 944-001 | Term names the inner cry and petition for divine justice — the persistent appeal of the wronged person to God for vindication |  |
| 1433 | 944-002 | Term names the execution of divine vengeance and justice — God's inner determination to right wrongs and vindicate the oppressed |  |
| 3618 | M26-A2-009 | Term names righteousness as something worn — it clothes, covers, and accompanies the person as an enveloping identity. Job put on righteousness and it clothed him; his justice was like a robe and turban (Job 29:14) — total coverage from head to body. Priests are to be clothed with righteousness (Psa 132:9) — those who serve before God are defined by wearing it. Paul commends the weapons of righteousness for the right hand and for the left (2Cor 6:7) — righteousness as active defensive and offensive equipment. Righteousness goes before the person and the glory of the Lord follows as rear guard (Isa 58:8) — righteousness as protective escort. The imagery conveys that righteousness is not one quality among others but the defining covering of the whole person's presence, identity, and movement. |  |

### G0159 aitios — causer (`mti_id=5`)

**Identity & meaning**

2. Registry: R073 guilt · Legacy C-cluster: C13

6. Mounce short-def: causer-

**Verses:** 5 distinct locations (see §2 for the verse table grouped by sub-group).

**Group memberships (1)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 3633 | M26-A1-007 | Term names righteousness as the defining quality of the coming Messianic figure — the Righteous One, the righteous Branch, the righteous Servant. The Servant's righteousness enables him to make many accounted righteous (Isa 53:11); the Branch is named 'The Lord is our righteousness' (Jer 23:6, 33:16); the Messianic king is 'righteous and having salvation' (Zec 9:9); Christ's love of righteousness grounds his anointing (Psa 45:7, Heb 1:9); he rides out victoriously for the cause of truth and meekness and righteousness (Psa 45:4). The prayer for the Messianic king's righteousness (Psa 72:2) is answered in Christ, and through the seventy weeks God brings in everlasting righteousness (Dan 9:24). Wisdom who walks in the paths of righteousness (Pro 8:20) and Melchizedek the king of righteousness (Heb 7:2) are types of this figure. In the NT, Jesus is identified as the Holy and Righteous One (Act 3:14), whose coming was betrayed (Act 7:52), who was appointed to be seen by his servant (Act 22:14). Even the Roman governor testifies to his innocence three times — I find no guilt in him (Joh 18:38, 19:4, 19:6; Luk 23:4, 14, 22; Act 13:28) — the pagan authority bears triple witness to Messianic righteousness. He died 'the righteous for the unrighteous' (1Pe 3:18), is called as advocate 'Jesus Christ the righteous' (1Jo 2:1), and as the source of eternal salvation to all who obey him (Heb 5:9). He is the telos of the law for righteousness (Rom 10:4). God's righteousness does not remain external — it becomes incarnate in the Messiah and through him is extended to all who believe. |  |

1. Strong's: `G2589` · Lang: Greek · Owner: OWNER

**Group memberships (1)**

| Group ID | Code | Description | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| 3628 | M26-A1-002 | Term names God's righteousness as expressed through his judicial acts — judgments that conform to what is right rather than to preference or power. God judges the world with righteousness (Psa 9:8), his throne gives righteous judgment (Psa 9:4), the heavens declare it (Psa 50:6), and his judgments are named just in their most severe eschatological expressions (Rev 15:3–19:2). Christ's judgment is just because it aligns with the Father's will (Joh 5:30). God's righteous rules are expressions of this judicial character (Psa 119:7, 75, 106). The law is holy and righteous and good (Rom 7:12); the commandments of God are so righteous that no other nation has statutes and rules as righteous (Deu 4:8); his testimonies are righteous forever and every righteous rule endures (Psa 119:144, 160). His righteous judgment is grounded in complete knowledge: he is the one who knows the hearts of all (Act 1:24, 15:8) — the judicial righteousness is not formal but penetrating. Every transgression and disobedience received a just retribution (Heb 2:2); their condemnation is just (Rom 3:8). |  |

| 3630 | M26-A1-004 | Term names God's righteousness as the basis on which he acts to save, deliver, vindicate, and redeem — the quality that makes him reliably and confidently appealable. The psalmists invoke it repeatedly as the ground of petition: 'In your righteousness deliver me' (Psa 31:1, 71:2, 143:1, 143:11). God's saving acts are expressions of his righteousness (1Sa 12:7). Righteousness and salvation travel inseparably (Isa 45:8, 46:13, 51:5). He vindicates the righteous by rewarding him according to his righteousness (1Ki 8:32, 2Ch 6:23); he restores to man his righteousness (Job 33:26); he brings forth righteousness as the light and justice as the noonday (Psa 37:6); justice returns to the righteous (Psa 94:15). The prayer of appeal is heard: standing before God's house and crying out — God answers for his name's sake (2Ch 20:9). In the NT, God's righteousness is the basis on which he forgives (1Jo 1:9), and the ground of the cross — where he is simultaneously just and the justifier (Rom 3:26). God gives his righteousness to his people as gift and vindication (Psa 24:5, Isa 54:17). |  |

| OBS-117-T2-001 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Isa 54:10, Num 6:26 | The peace registry reveals that shalom is constitutive of God's identity, not merely a condition he produces. The theological anchor terms (H3073 YHWH-Shalom, H3071 YHWH-Nissi, H3072 YHWH-Tsidkenu) further confirm: God names himself by his characteristic acts and identities, and peace is one of those constitutive names. |
| OBS-117-T2-002 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Num 6:26, Psa 4:8, Isa 26:3, Jer 29:11 | Yes — extensively. God possesses shalom as identity (YHWH-Shalom, OBS-117-148, OBS-117-001), gives shalom covenantally (Num 6:26, Isa 54:10, OBS-117-145), makes shalom in his high heaven (Job 25:2, prior session OBS-117-103), plans shalom for his people (Jer 29:11, OBS-117-149), and speaks peace to his saints (Psa 85:8, OBS-117-145). The significance for the human person: because shalom is grounded in God's own identity, the human inner condition of peace is not self-generated but participatory — the human person receives and inhabits a peace that belongs to God. Psa 4:8 ("for you alone, O Lord, make me dwell in safety") is the clearest statement: the shalom of the inner life depends entirely on God as its ground. Isa 26:3 (OBS-117-144) confirms: perfect peace for the mind stayed on God — the directionality is always from God-as-peace-source to human-as-peace-recipient. |
| OBS-117-T2-003 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Mal 2:6, Psa 4:8, Lam 3:17, Jer 29:11 | Peace serves as the integrative condition of the human person's existence — the state in which the person is whole, functional, and in right relation with God, self, and others. The evidence from OBS-117-144, OBS-117-149, OBS-117-156 establishes this: shalom as wholeness of person (Isa 26:3 — mind, trust, peace as an integrated state), welfare of the whole person (Jer 29:11 — future, hope), soul's rest (Psa 4:8). The purpose is not merely psychological comfort but existential integrity — peace is the condition in which the person fulfils their creaturely purpose of walking with God (Mal 2:6, OBS-117-146: walked with me in peace and uprightness). Lam 3:17 ("my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is") shows by negation: without shalom, the soul loses access to its own flourishing. |
| OBS-117-T2-006 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 120:7, Isa 26:3, Jer 29:7, Mal 2:6 | Peace expresses the divine image in two ways: (1) Relational shalom — the human person's orientation toward peace with others (H7965G 2508-003: "I am for peace," Psa 120:7; Mal 2:6: walked in peace and uprightness; OBS-117-146) mirrors God's own relational orientation of peace toward his people (2508-002, OBS-117-145). The peacemaker (H7965J 2510-001 — seeking the sha.lom of others) images the God who plans sha.lom for his people (Jer 29:7/11). (2) Inner peace as the God-oriented condition — Isa 26:3 (OBS-117-144) establishes that perfect peace is the condition of the mind directed toward God. This is not a neutral human achievement but an image-bearing posture: the human person who is oriented toward God-as-peace bears that peace as an inner condition. The divine image expressed through peace is the capacity for integrative wholeness — body, soul, relationships, and God all in right order. |
| OBS-117-T2-007 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Isa 26:3, Mic 5:5, Isa 54:10 | Shared — and with the unique feature that the sharing is constitutive rather than merely analogical. The evidence distinguishes: (1) God IS peace (YHWH-Shalom, 406-001, OBS-117-148) — divine ontological possession; (2) the human person receives peace from God and inhabits it (2508-001, OBS-117-144: soul bereft of peace; perfect peace for the trusting mind, Isa 26:3); (3) the messianic king IS peace (2508-004, Mic 5:5, OBS-117-147) — in the DHC structure, the divine-human boundary is breached in the direction of identification, not analogy. The peace that the human receives is not an analogue of divine peace but a participation in it — received from the same source (God), grounded in the same reality (covenant), moving toward the same telos (eschatological wholeness). This is a stronger connection than analogy; it is participation. The peace the human person bears is genuinely the same peace that God IS, received through covenant. |
| OBS-117-T2-008 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Psa 119:165, Mal 2:6, Deu 29:19, Isa 48:22, Psa 38:3 | The data provides clear evidence in both directions. Mal 2:6 (OBS-117-146) — walking in peace and uprightness — is the portrait of the image-bearing priest. Absence: the absence of shalom is diagnostic of inner disorder. Three patterns emerge: (1) Moral disorder → absence of peace: Isa 48:22/57:21 ("there is no peace for the wicked"); the way of peace unknown to those in moral disorder (Isa 59:8); (2) False peace as image corruption: Deu 29:19 (2509-002, OBS-117-150) — the stubborn-hearted person who claims shalom without covenant ground; Jer 6:14/8:11 — prophets crying "peace, peace" when there is no peace; the corrupted image produces false shalom claims; (3) Somatic evidence: Psa 38:3 ("no health in my bones because of my sin") — the absence of shalom-wholeness has bodily expression. The image-bearing conclusion: shalom's presence is the marker of the person in right order with God; its absence (or false claim) reveals image-corruption at the deepest level. |
| OBS-117-T2-009 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Isa 9:6, Isa 53:5, Mic 5:5 | Yes — more explicitly than most characteristics in the programme. The typological structure is built into the vocabulary itself: YHWH-Shalom (Judg 6:24, 406-001) → Prince of Peace (Isa 9:6, 2508-004) → He shall be their peace (Mic 5:5, 2508-004) → He himself is our peace (Eph 2:14, G1515 group 426-003). Each stage names the same "X IS peace" ontological structure at a different historical moment. Isa 53:5 ("the chastisement that brought us peace... with his wounds we are healed") is the most compressed typological moment: the Servant's suffering produces peace as its direct result — and the NT reader recognises this as the mechanism of the peace Christ IS. The typology is not imposed on the text but inhabits it — the peace vocabulary itself carries the forward-pointing structure. DIM-117-SD003 (raised in DimReview session) targets this exactly. |
| OBS-117-T2-011 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Eph 2:14 | The fulfilment of peace in Christ reveals that the original design was for the human person to live in the peace of God — not a condition achieved by the person but a Person known. The "YHWH-Shalom" altar name (406-001) names God as peace in the context of a theophany — the human person encountering God, receiving peace, and naming God as its source and substance. The fulfilment in Christ (Eph 2:14 — he himself is our peace) reveals that this was always the design: peace is not an abstract inner state but a relational reality grounded in the presence of the one who IS peace. The original design for the human person was to dwell in God's peace through covenant fellowship — and the NT peace in Christ is the restoration and fulfilment of that original design. The "dividing wall of hostility" broken down (Eph 2:14) is not a new problem solved but an original rupture healed. |
| OBS-117-T2-012 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Judg 6:24, Isa 9:6, Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8 | The primary Hebrew name is shalom (sha.lom, H7965G and sub-forms) — the most semantically wide term in the OT inner-being vocabulary. Its breadth is encoded in the STEP gloss: completeness, soundness, welfare, peace, quiet, tranquillity, contentment, friendship, covenant peace, peace from war (OBS-117-009). The name itself reveals that peace is not a simple state but a structural condition of completeness — everything in its proper place. Secondary Hebrew names: nachat (na.chat — quietness/quietude, H5183A), shaqat (sha.qat — to quiet/be quiet, H8252), nuach (nu.ach — rest, H5117), menuchah (me.nu.chah — resting place, H4496H). The primary Greek name is eirēnē (G1515) — which in the LXX became the translation word for shalom, absorbing its full range. The sub-names (YHWH-Shalom: "LORD is peace"; Prince of Peace: sar shalom) reveal that peace is not merely a human condition but a divine identity: the name carries what the named one IS. |
| OBS-117-T2-014 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Jer 29:11, Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8 | The core inner-being essence of shalom is integrative wholeness — the condition of the whole person (body, soul, mind, relationships, and orientation toward God) in which everything is in its proper place and functioning rightly. This is not a narrow emotional state but an existential condition. The data establishes this through convergence: (1) The etymology of shalom (completeness, soundness — OBS-117-009) names the absence of fragmentation or deficiency; (2) Lam 3:17 (OBS-117-144) — "my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is" — shows that shalom-deprivation is total inner destitution, not merely sadness; (3) Jer 29:11 (OBS-117-149) — "welfare... future... hope" — the three outcomes of God's sha.lom-intention show peace as the condition that makes flourishing possible; (4) Isa 26:3 (OBS-117-144) — perfect peace for the mind stayed on God — shows that shalom is the integrative outcome of right orientation. The essence is best stated: peace is the condition of integrative wholeness in which the person is complete, secure, and in right relation — capable of their full creaturely purpose. |
| OBS-117-T2-016 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 48:22, Deu 29:19, Amo 6:1, Isa 26:3 | The negative definition is richly evidenced: (1) Peace is not the absence of war alone — the shalom vocabulary covers inner states that have nothing to do with military conflict (Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8, Lam 3:17); (2) Peace is not self-generated security — the false shalom pattern (2509-002, Deu 29:19) shows that self-claimed peace without covenant ground is its precise corruption; (3) Peace is not available to the wicked — Isa 48:22, 57:21 ("there is no peace for the wicked"); the moral character of the person determines whether shalom is accessible; (4) Peace is not silence or passivity — the silence vocabulary (H2790B, H2814, G4623) is distinct from shalom; silence is a condition that enables or expresses peace but is not peace itself; (5) Peace is not complacent ease — sha'anan (H7600 group 413-001) names self-generated ease as a vice (Amo 6:1: "woe to those who are at ease in Zion"), demonstrating that ease without divine ground is not shalom. The negative boundary: peace is not a condition the person achieves by themselves, claims without God's ground, or inhabits while in moral disorder. |
| OBS-117-T2-018 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Jer 6:14, Deu 29:19, Psa 28:3 | Yes — and this is among the most analytically significant features of the peace registry. The false-peace pattern (2508-003 and 2509-002) shows that shalom is precisely the characteristic that can be falsely claimed: (1) False prophecy of shalom: Jer 6:14, 8:11, Eze 13:10, 13:16, Mic 3:5 — prophets cry "Peace, peace" when there is no peace (prior session, incorporated as source material); (2) Self-deceptive shalom: Deu 29:19 (2509-002, OBS-117-150) — "I shall be safe, though I walk in the stubbornness of my heart"; (3) Complacent ease (sha'anan, H7600 group 413-001) — the comfortable feel of worldly security mistaken for shalom; (4) Speaking shalom with evil in the heart: Psa 28:3, Jer 9:8 — "speaks peace to his neighbor while evil is in his heart." The implication: shalom is the most counterfeitable of inner-being characteristics because its absence-of-distress phenomenology can be replicated by complacency, self-deception, or false prophecy. Authentic shalom requires its proper ground (God, covenant, trust) while false shalom is the same phenomenological condition without that ground. |
| OBS-117-T2-020 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 30:15, 1Pe 3:4, Isa 32:18 | The data shows a rich cognate field. Hebrew near-synonyms functioning alongside shalom: (1) Nuach/menuchah (rest, resting-place) — the cessation-dimension of peace; (2) Nachat (H5183A — quietness) — the inner stillness dimension, co-occurs with shaqat in Isa 30:15 at the analytical summit; (3) Shaqat (H8252 — quiet/quieted) — the active quieting, most prominent in its paired form "quietness and trust" (Isa 30:15); (4) Sha'anan (H7600 — at ease) — the ease dimension, positively (Isa 32:18) and negatively (Amo 6:1); (5) Demamah (H1827 — profound silence) — the threshold silence before divine speech. Greek near-synonyms: eirēnē (G1515) is not a synonym but the translation equivalent; hēsuchia/hēsuchios (G2271/G2272) — quietness, the NT equivalent of the Hebrew silence vocabulary (1Pe 3:4 — gentle and quiet spirit); anesis (G0425) — relief/rest from pressure. The cognate field reveals that peace has an inner-face (the silence/stillness vocabulary) and an outer-face (the welfare/wholeness vocabulary), and shalom is the integrative term that spans both. |
| OBS-117-T2-021 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Psa 120:7, Jer 29:11, Isa 45:7, Jer 30:5, Deu 29:19 | The data provides explicit antonyms: (1) War (milchamah) — Psa 120:7 ("I am for peace, but when I speak, they are for war"); the most direct antonym in the moral-orientation register; (2) Evil (ra'ah) — Jer 29:11 ("plans for welfare and not for evil"); the antonym in the divine-intention register; (3) Calamity/disaster — Isa 45:7 ("I make well-being and create calamity"); the antonym in the creational register; (4) Terror/panic — Jer 30:5 ("a cry of panic, of terror, and no peace"); the experiential antonym; (5) Stubbornness/iniquity — Deu 29:19, Mal 2:6 ("turned many from iniquity"); moral disorder as peace's contrary; (6) Anguish — Eze 7:25 ("when anguish comes they will seek peace, but there shall be none"). The antonym profile confirms that peace is not a neutral absence of distress but a positive condition with multiple contraries — war (relational), evil (moral), calamity (existential), terror (experiential), stubbornness (dispositional). |
| OBS-117-T2-022 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 53:5, Isa 38:17, Amo 6:1, Isa 32:18 | Yes — three paradoxes are documented: (1) Peace through suffering: Isa 53:5 ("the chastisement that brought us peace, and with his wounds we are healed") — peace is purchased through suffering, not despite it (OBS-117-147). The mechanism of peace is its opposite (pain, punishment); (2) Peace through bitterness: Isa 38:17 ("it was for my welfare [shalom] that I had great bitterness") — Hezekiah's retrospective: his suffering was the path to his welfare. The paradox: shalom-welfare arrived via its experiential opposite; (3) Complacent ease as false peace: H7600 group 413-001 (Amo 6:1) and group 413-002 (Isa 32:18) — the same experiential condition of ease is either vice (self-generated) or blessing (divinely given). Peace and its counterfeit may be phenomenologically indistinguishable. The paradoxes converge on a single insight: authentic shalom is not produced by the absence of suffering or difficulty but by the presence of the peace-giver. Its opposite conditions (suffering, bitterness, difficulty) can be the means by which it arrives. |
| OBS-117-T2-024 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Deu 28:65, Eze 7:25, Job 3:26 | Documented immediate responses to shalom's absence: (1) Soul bereft — Lam 3:17 ("my soul is bereft of peace; I have forgotten what happiness is") — total inner destitution, loss of access to happiness itself; (2) Bones wasting — Psa 32:3 (prior session: "when I kept silent, my bones wasted away through my groaning all day long") — somatic deterioration; (3) Trembling heart — Deu 28:65 ("a trembling heart and failing eyes and a languishing soul") — the triple somatic-inner expression of peacelessness in judgment; (4) Anguish seeking — Eze 7:25 ("when anguish comes they will seek peace, but there shall be none"); (5) Job's triple negation — Job 3:26 ("I am not at ease, nor am I quiet... I have no rest, but trouble comes"). The withdrawal of shalom is experienced as total inner collapse — not discomfort but destitution. The soul's access to its own capacity for happiness is severed (Lam 3:17). |
| OBS-117-T2-025 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 28:12, Jer 6:16, Isa 30:15 | Yes — the refusal of peace pattern is one of the most analytically significant in the registry. The data shows peace offered and refused: (1) Isa 28:12 ("This is rest, give rest to the weary... but they would not hear") — rest/peace offered and refused by Israel; (2) Jer 6:16 ("stand by the roads, and look, and ask for the ancient paths, where the good way is; and walk in it, and find rest for your souls. But you were unwilling") — the most concentrated statement of peace refused. The inability to receive peace is not ignorance but refusal — the will actively declines the condition. This pattern is concentrated in the OT prophets and names a structural feature of the human condition: peace is available but resisted. |
| OBS-117-T2-027 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | R117 peace | Psa 38:3, Deu 28:65, Jer 12:12 | Sustained absence of shalom produces progressive deterioration: (1) Somatic degradation — Psa 38:3 ("no soundness in my flesh... no health in my bones because of my sin") — sustained peacelessness shows in the body; (2) Exile and homelessness — Deu 28:65 ("you shall find no respite, and there shall be no resting place for the sole of your foot") — sustained peacelessness produces the ultimate displacement; (3) Universal desolation — Jer 12:12 ("no flesh has peace") — when shalom is withdrawn from a community, the deprivation becomes total; (4) The wicked's false prosperity — Job 21:9 ("their houses are safe from fear") reveals by contrast: the wicked have external safety but not genuine shalom — the sustained absence of authentic peace produces the counterfeit of complacent ease. The trajectory: inner → somatic → communal → eschatological. |
| OBS-117-T2-029 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Psa 4:8, Psa 119:165, Isa 26:3, Phili 4:7 | Yes — as established in Q&A-020. The data shows: absent (Lam 3:17 — soul bereft), present (Psa 4:8 — lying down in peace), great (Psa 119:165 — great peace for law-lovers), perfect (Isa 26:3 — shalom shalom for the trust-directed mind), eschatological-unlimited (Isa 9:7 — no end to his peace). | OBS-117-T2-032 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 54:10, Num 6:26, Isa 26:3, Psa 4:8, Isa 9:6, Mic 5:5, Eph 2:14 | The most important single finding from T1: peace (shalom/eirēnē) is not primarily an emotional state or an absence of conflict, but an integrative wholeness grounded in divine identity and structured through covenant. The evidence establishes four dimensions that are not alternatives but a sequence: (1) Covenantal ground: shalom exists because God covenants it — the covenant of peace (Isa 54:10) is the basis; (2) Relational gift: God gives shalom to those in covenant (Num 6:26), speaks it to his saints (Psa 85:8), plans it for his people (Jer 29:11); (3) Inner condition: the received shalom becomes the experienced condition of the whole person — perfect peace for the trust-directed mind (Isa 26:3), soul at rest (Psa 4:8), welfare of the body and soul (Psa 38:3, 55:18); (4) Eschatological telos: the peace given and experienced is not complete until it is embodied in a Person — the Prince of Peace (Isa 9:6), he who shall be their peace (Mic 5:5), he who himself is our peace (Eph 2:14). DIM-117-002 (the pending finding about the nature of shalom) is hereby resolved: shalom is all four dimensions simultaneously, as a structured and connected reality. The central analytical finding is the sequence: divine identity → covenant gift → inner condition → eschatological embodiment in a Person. [RESOLVING DIM-117-002] |
| OBS-117-T2-033 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace |  | Two genuine gaps: (1) Pre-fall peace: the data does not address what shalom was in the original creation before the fall. This is a structural gap in the lexical approach: the peace vocabulary emerges in a fallen world and is therefore always shaped by covenant-restoration; (2) The relationship between the silence/quietness vocabulary (the "inner face" of peace) and shalom itself: the rest/quiet/silence Hebrew terms (H5117, H4496H, H5183A, H8252, and others) form the large majority of the registry's groups, yet their relationship to shalom is not formally defined in the data — they co-occur and co-occur in the same theological contexts but the data does not explicitly state whether shalom produces silence/quiet or silence/quiet produces shalom (though Isa 30:15 — quietness and trust = strength — suggests the latter). Gap deferred to Session D. |

| OBS-117-T2-039 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Lam 3:17, Psa 55:18, Psa 62:5, Psa 131:2, Jer 6:16 | Soul-level location establishes peace as a fundamental condition of the whole inner person's experience — not a cognitive state (mind-level) or a moral quality (character-level) but the experiential condition of the nephesh as living, feeling, wanting creature. The nephesh is the person as they are most fully themselves — most vulnerable, most alive, most in need. Locating shalom in the nephesh means: (1) When peace is present, the whole person flourishes (Psa 55:18 — soul in safety); (2) When peace is absent, the whole person is destitute (Lam 3:17 — soul bereft, happiness forgotten); (3) Peace can be commanded to the soul (Psa 62:5, 131:2) — the soul can be directed toward peace by addressing it as a subject. This last point is analytically significant: the soul is not passive in relation to peace but can be called to orientation. The soul-level location means peace is the deepest personal experiential reality — the condition in which the self-as-person rests or is bereft. |

| OBS-117-T2-042 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Phili 4:7, Rom 8:6 | Yes — explicitly and centrally. The primary mind-location: Isa 26:3 ("You keep him in perfect peace whose mind [yetser — formed thought/disposition] is stayed on you, because he trusts in you") — OBS-117-144. This is the most direct mind-peace statement in the corpus: perfect peace (shalom shalom) is the condition of the mind that is directed toward and stayed upon God. The yetser is the formed inclination or disposition of the mind — its sustained directedness. In the NT: Phili 4:7 — "the peace of God... will guard your hearts and your minds [noema — thoughts, understanding]" — the mind is jointly named as the territory peace guards alongside the heart; Rom 8:6 — "to set the mind [phronema] on the Spirit is life and peace" — the mind's orientation determines whether peace is present. The mind is the second major constitutional location of peace after the soul, and the most explicitly causal: directing the mind toward God produces peace; directing the mind elsewhere forfeits it. |
| OBS-117-T2-043 | THEOLOGICAL_NOTE | R117 peace | Isa 26:3, Rom 8:6, Phili 4:7 | The mind-peace connection primarily engages the mind's directional function — the capacity of the mind to fix itself upon an object. Isa 26:3 (yetser) — the "stayed" or "fixed" mind: peace requires the mind to be anchored, not drifting. The mental act that produces peace is not thought-content (what the mind thinks about) but mental orientation (what the mind is stayed upon). Rom 8:6 (phronema — the mind's settled disposition/inclination) — the mind's habitual orientation toward the Spirit produces peace; toward the flesh produces death. Phili 4:7 (noema — the products of thought, understanding) — the mind's thoughts are what peace guards: peace stands sentinel over the mind's outputs. Three aspects: (1) Orientation (Isa 26:3) — where the mind is directed; (2) Disposition (Rom 8:6) — what the mind is habitually inclined toward; (3) Output (Phili 4:7) — the contents of thought that peace protects. The mind's peace is a total engagement — orientation, disposition, and output are all in view. |

Peace at this level is not performance but being. | OBS-117-T2-046 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | R117 peace | Psa 32:3, Psa 38:3, Deu 28:65, Psa 4:8, Isa 53:5 | Yes — and this is one of the most extensive somatic evidence sets in the programme. | OBS-117-T2-047 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | R117 peace | Psa 38:3, Deu 28:65, Psa 4:8, Isa 53:5, Psa 32:3 | The body-part links serve multiple functions: (1) Indicative — the body's condition indicates the inner-being condition: Psa 38:3 (no soundness in flesh/no health in bones because of sin) — the body reads out the inner state of peacelessness; the body is a diagnostic display of the inner condition; (2) Emphatic — Deu 28:65 accumulates three somatic signals (trembling heart, failing eyes, languishing soul) to intensify the portrait of peacelessness in judgment; the body amplifies the inner condition; (3) Expressive — Psa 4:8 (lying down and sleeping) — the body enacts peace by doing what peace enables; sleep is the body's expression of inner security; (4) Mediating — Isa 53:5 ("with his wounds we are healed") — the Servant's body mediates peace to others; the physical wounds are the mechanism of the relational restoration. The most significant body-link is Psa 38:3: the explicit naming of shalom as soundness-of-bones under sin's influence establishes that inner peace and somatic integrity are structurally connected — not metaphorically but causally. |
| OBS-117-T2-048 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | R117 peace | Psa 38:3, Psa 32:3, Isa 53:5, Psa 4:8 | Both directions are evidenced, with the dominant direction being inner-state → body: (1) Inner → body (primary direction): Psa 38:3 — sin disrupts inner peace → no health in bones; Psa 32:3 — suppressed confession (inner withholding) → bones wasting; Deu 28:65 — peacelessness in judgment → trembling heart, failing eyes, languishing soul. The inner condition of un-peace expresses itself through somatic deterioration. (2) Body → inner: Isa 53:5 ("with his wounds we are healed") — the Servant's somatic wounds produce inner healing/peace for others; the body of the Servant becomes the mechanism of inner restoration for the many. This is the most theologically significant body→inner direction in the corpus — a body bearing suffering that produces inner peace in others. (3) Body expressing inner: Psa 4:8 — the body lying down and sleeping expresses an already-present inner peace; the body does not produce peace but enacts it. The primary causal direction is inner → body; the Isa 53:5 direction (body → inner) is the unique redemptive reversal. |
| OBS-117-T2-049 | SOMATIC_EVIDENCE | R117 peace | Psa 38:3, Isa 53:5 | The primary direction (inner → body) establishes peace as a genuinely somatic characteristic — its absence or presence registers in the body because the body is the outward expression of the inner state. This has two consequences: (1) Peace cannot be fully present in the inner person while the body is in somatic distress caused by inner disorder — the body is a truth-telling indicator of the inner condition (Psa 38:3, 32:3); a person who claims inner peace while their body deteriorates under the weight of unconfessed sin is in the false-shalom pattern; (2) The redemptive reversal (Isa 53:5 body → inner) is extraordinary precisely because it runs against the normal causal direction: normally inner disorder produces somatic deterioration; in the Servant's case, somatic suffering produces inner healing in others. The directionality finding deepens the T1 paradox (Q&A-023): peace comes through its opposite conditions not only experientially but physically. |
| OBS-117-T2-050 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace | Psa 38:3, Pro 3:2 | The evidence provides indirect but not conclusive evidence for a bodily deposit. What is present: (1) Psa 38:3 and 32:3 establish that sustained absence of peace (inner disorder) produces somatic deterioration — bones wasting, no soundness in flesh. This implies that sustained presence of peace should produce the inverse — somatic health and soundness; (2) Pro 3:2 ("length of days and years of life and peace they will add to you") — wisdom-and-peace produces length of life; shalom is associated with longevity as its somatic fruit; (3) Pro 3:17 ("all her paths are peace") — wisdom's characteristic product is peace as a sustained life-condition. The wisdom literature implies that sustained shalom has somatic extension effects (length of life). However: the data does not explicitly address constitutional deposit in the technical sense (DNA, generational transmission). The silence is methodological rather than substantive — the biblical vocabulary does not operate with the category of genetic or physiological deposit. Gap: the question of generational or developmental deposit for sustained peace is not addressable from this data. This finding feeds directly into T5.7. |
| OBS-117-T2-051 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace | Pro 3:2, Psa 38:3, 1Th 5:23 | Supporting: Pro 3:2 (peace associated with longer life — a life-outcome that implies somatic extension); the somatic-deterioration pattern under sustained peacelessness (Psa 38:3, 32:3, Deu 28:65) implies by negation that sustained peace protects somatic integrity. Contradicting: no verse explicitly claims that shalom leaves a genetic or physiological deposit. The closest is Isa 66:12 ("like a river... like an overflowing stream") — the image of peace as overflowing suggests abundance rather than deposit. Neutral: 1Th 5:23 (the God of peace sanctifying the whole person — spirit, soul, body) suggests peace's reach is total but does not specify deposit mechanisms. The evidence supports somatic consequence of peace (in both directions) but not constitutional deposit in the genetic/biological sense. Gap explicitly noted for T5.7. |
| OBS-117-T2-052 | OBSERVATION | R117 peace |  | The evidence is partially silent on constitutional deposit in the technical sense. The data establishes somatic consequence (in both directions) but not deposit.
